Spring 1624 — First Blood on the Croquant Frontier

April–May 1624 · resolved at the table 28–31 August 2020 · Mission: Defence · Theater: the frontier works, against the Croquant rebels

The game’s first campaign, and its first grave. Two men volunteered for the spring war against the Croquant peasant rebellion; one came back. Everything below is quoted verbatim from the table record.

The situation

The rules of war had been posted before anyone marched. The Narrator’s standing order for volunteers:

Narrator — “Summer is the regular campaign season, when the Minister of War will call up a certain number of brigades and send them to the front. Characters may also volunteer to campaign in the other seasons (Fall, Winter, Spring), along with their whole command if they hold one. […] Characters without a command may take a temporary leave-of-absence to serve in a Frontier Regiment. Characters retain their rank, or receive the rank of Private if they were not enlisted, and are paid at Gascon Regiment rates.”

Why would a freshly commissioned captain of the Picardy Musketeers leave Paris for the frontier in spring? Guillaume d’Avergny told his friends plainly, in the streets, in April 1624 — rejected in love, broke, and proud:

Guillaume d’Avergny — “My friends, I’ve a confession. These past two months have been the most exciting and engaging of my life. Even so, a specter haunts me, hanging over my every thought and deed.

In truth, Mlle. Giteau’s rejection was more than I could bear – not just emotionally, but financially. I cannot remain in Paris and stay solvent. And now that I carry the honor of my regiment on my shoulders, as well as that of the d’Avergnys, I can ill afford to Disgrace my name.

I will be volunteering for the frontier on the first of the month. I’ll see you on the other side of the war.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — “Au revoir monsieur d’Avergny! May the frontier reward you with opportunities for glory!”

Pierre de Tête — “Stay safe, my friend. Let me lend you some reading materials. There is no culture at the frontier.”

Phillipe du Kaneda — “Remember the wisdom of your cousin and his hatchet. There is no honor in dying to those with none.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “Make sure to bring us stories of the frontier when you return!”

He would not return. On 28 August 2020 (May 1624 in game time) the Narrator opened the campaign:

Narrator — “@Jules Lavelle and @DancingHorse have volunteered for the Spring campaign against the Croquant rebels.. Guillaume has joined the Frontier Regiments, but Pierre has volunteered with his 3rd Squadron of Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “The Spring campaign will revolve around Defence.”

Pierre de Tête — “Those peasants don’t stand a chance against a proper soldier!”

Narrator — “Campaign will be resolved at the end of the month.”

The order of battle

A small, lonely war — no grand army, no brigades, no famous generals yet. The Narrator’s entire order of battle for the defence:

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiment is operating independently on this defensive campaign. The Colonel leading the Frontier Regiment has an MA of 5. His adjutant has an MA of 2.”

The player characters engaged:

  • Guillaume d’Avergny — junior captain of the Picardy Musketeers, on leave-of-absence with the Frontier Regiments, posted to the front line of the defensive works. (Guillaume was, as it happens, the Narrator’s own character — the GM’s player account. The dice showed him no courtesy for it.)
  • Pierre de Tête — junior major, Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons, volunteering at the head of his own 3rd Squadron.

Facing them: the Croquant peasant leagues in revolt, under the rebel leaders Donat and Barran.

The record

The month passed first in tedium. One letter survives from the works, and one reply from Paris:

Guillaume d’Avergny — “This is the worst food I’ve ever had in my life. Why did I think war would be glorious? So far I’ve sat behind fortifications and tried not to use my nose.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “Ah yes, war” … “Two weeks of extreme boredom followed by 10 minutes of sheer terror”

On 31 August 2020 the Narrator called the session to order:

Narrator — “Campaign Resolution”

Narrator — “The battle begins! Croquant rebels assault the position. The colonel rolls:”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “Battle result of 5”

(A disaster. At the table, the Narrator’s own verdict on the colonel’s roll: “pure crap … Pierre has a chance of salvaging it a bit, I’m screwed.”)

Narrator — “@DancingHorse , roll 1d6 to determine the result for your 3rd Squadron.”

Pierre de Tête — “With valiant heart, I order my captain to roll!”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 1d6 — 6

Pierre de Tête — “Promote that man!”

Narrator — “The colonel’s orders are delivered by a muddy, shoeless child. Pierre reviews them, and recognizes the defensive plan for the sheer idiocy it is. As rebels pour over the battlements, Pierre leads his Squadron on a fighting withdrawal. As the rest of the defensive works fall, Pierre’s command alone rides to safety.”

The reckoning

Pierre de Tête — the fighting withdrawal

Narrator — “Pierre, roll 2d6 for Death against a 12. You may first decide to demonstrate Poltroonery or Reckless Bravery if you wish to modify the rolls”

DancingHorse (checking) — “To clarify, a 12 =death?”

Narrator — “yes, for each of these, you must roll at or above for it to happen”

Pierre de Tête — “A cool head prevails, no need for Reckless Bravery needed and Poltroonery is never an option!”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — (2+6) = 8 (Death target 12: survives)

Narrator — “Pierre survives!”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — (1+4) = 5 (Mentioned in Despatches target 12: no) 🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — (5+3) = 8 (Promotion target 10: no) 🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — (3+4) = 7 (Crowns target 9: no)

Narrator — “Pierre returns from the front having survived, but failed to distinguish himself in any other way”

Narrator — “Pierre, for this month you will receive pay as though you were a captain in the Gascon Regiment”

WillWorker (at the table) — “Lieutenant D. Golém strikes again!”

Guillaume d’Avergny — the front line

Narrator — “Now, @Jules Lavelle…”

Guillaume d’Avergny — “Is this the pale hand of Death before me?”

Narrator — “Guillaume is part of the front line of defenses. The battlement before him blows apart in the first rebel onslaught, and his position is quickly overwhelmed.”

Narrator — “Roll against a 6 for Death.”

etirflita (at the table) — “oof”

🎲 Guillaume d’Avergny rolls 2d6 — (6+4) = 10 (Death target 6: dies) ☠️☠️

Narrator — “Guillaume is annihilated”

Pierre de Tête — “Guillauuuuuuuummmmme!”

The first player character death of the game — three weeks in, and it was the Narrator’s own. The cemetery channel opened that evening with a single image:

The defeat, and what it bought

Narrator — “The frontier regiments have suffered a crushing defeat at the hands of the rebel leaders Donat and Barran. Their names are growing in popularity, and the rebellion threatens to spread as nearby peasantry wonder if they too can escape the ruinous tax burdens of the King.”

Narrator — “The King charges the Minister of War to raise a great army to quell the rebellion once and for all.”

No honors, no promotions, no plunder. One captain’s pay-packet, one corpse, and a summer of total war now guaranteed for every man in Paris.

Aftermath in the streets

Minutes after the death roll, back in the streets of Paris — Jean-Luc Aveline had not yet heard:

Jean-Luc Aveline — “When @Jules Lavelle gets home from the front, I owe him a drink…”

Narrator — “Guillaume’s sword is returned from the front and set aside in the post office for his family to claim. It sits in the corner for years, rusting away, before it is finally thrown away during a renovation.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — “empty chairs at empty tables.... at Red Phillip’s“

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “The pallor of death hangs over the city this day.”

That same evening, the survivor rode in through the gates:

Pierre de Tête — “Ahh, the city, a haven of civilization! And womenfolk! A sight for sore eyes after a month on weary campaign. I’ll need a drink.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — “@DancingHorse, it seems being on campaign has served you well; I suspect you are back to full health after our ‘tavern scuffle’ at the Frog and Peach?”

Pierre de Tête — “Nearly, those were some deep cuts.”

And before midnight, a new gentleman appeared in the streets — the Narrator’s next character, arriving with the ink of the death notice barely dry:

Jules Lavelle — “Finally, I have convinced my father to permit me freedom in determining my own affairs. The old fool imagines the world still runs as though we were under Charlemagne. But no, it is a new age and a new day for Jules Lavelle!”

Narrator — “Spring 1624 draws to a close. Characters in Paris, submit your actions for June.”

Table talk

The tempting of fate, three days before the resolution:

  • Jules Lavelle (GM, playing Guillaume) — “Dice Golem is nothing if not punctual”
  • etirflita — “So Dice Golem is ‘nothing’ now? Tempting fate, aren’t we Guillaume?”
  • WillWorker — “Bah, he’s on the front lines. What could Dice Golem possibly do to him now....”
  • Jules Lavelle — “Dice Golem can finally KILL”

And the eulogies, such as they were:

  • WillWorker — “Eh, fuck ‘em. I didn’t like the guy anyways.” / Jules Lavelle — “he was kind of a stick-in-the-mud”
  • ArcticFox — “On the bright side, you get to roll a new character!” / Jules Lavelle — “Yep looking forward to it”
  • etirflita — “This past campaign was a big advertisement for going on campaigns.” / Jules Lavelle — “That’s why it’s not a choice in the summer”
  • Jules Lavelle (on the odds for the coming summer, after suggesting poltroonery) — “Discretion is the better part of valor. Cowardice is the better part of discretion” / WillWorker — “You spelled pragmatism wrong.”
❦

Summer 1624 — The Great Campaign against the Croquants

June–August 1624 · resolved at the table 31 August – 10 September 2020 · Mission: Field Operations & Defence · Theater: the Croquant rebel country

The situation

The Spring campaign had ended in catastrophe: the Frontier Regiments broken on their own battlements, and Guillaume d’Avergny dead in the first onslaught. On the evening of 31 August the Narrator set the stakes for the summer:

Narrator — “The frontier regiments have suffered a crushing defeat at the hands of the rebel leaders Donat and Barran. Their names are growing in popularity, and the rebellion threatens to spread as nearby peasantry wonder if they too can escape the ruinous tax burdens of the King.”

Narrator — “The King charges the Minister of War to raise a great army to quell the rebellion once and for all.”

What, precisely, the peasants were rebelling over was only named aloud in the streets of Paris after the army came home — a salt tax:

Jules Lavelle — “Where have you been, my good man! It’s been all the news. Filthy Croquants, risen again, put down again. The same as it ever was. … Apparently fretting over tax issues, like common money-grubbing swine. A salt tax, of all the preposterous notions. They live without art, culture, soap, but ask for a bit of their salt and it’s an uprising all at once.”

The order of battle

The Minister of War rolled to determine the size of the levy:

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “A great campaign has been called. The following brigades will be sent: Guards Brigade / Heavy Brigade / Dragoon Brigade / 2nd Foot Brigade / 3rd Foot Brigade / 4th Foot Brigade”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4 (the Minister’s disposition of forces)

Narrator — “The army will attempt to defend against further encroachment while performing raids on the enemy when opportunity arises. … Assignments: Guards, Heavy, Dragoon Brigades: Field Operations — 2nd, 3rd, 4th Foot Brigades: Defence.”

Three armies were deployed. Every player character then living was called up except Curtis Sinnoch, Alphonse Devereux, Pain Loafmann, and the newly arrived Jules Lavelle (“Guillaume would not have been deployed, of course,” the Narrator added). The player characters stood as follows:

Character Rank / post Unit Formation
Jean-Luc Aveline Private Archduke Leopold Cuirassiers Heavy Brigade, Cavalry Division, 1st Army
Pierre de Tête Commander, 3rd Squadron Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons Dragoon Brigade, Cavalry Division, 1st Army
Godefroy de Beaufosse Trooper Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons Dragoon Brigade, Cavalry Division, 1st Army
Etienne de Mercoeur Guardsman Cardinal’s Guard Guards Brigade, 1st Army
Leon Mandelbrot Subaltern 53rd Fusiliers 2nd Brigade of Foot, 1st Division, 2nd Army
Phillipe du Kaneda Private 53rd Fusiliers 2nd Brigade of Foot, 1st Division, 2nd Army

The commanders above them, as the Narrator introduced each in turn during the battle: General Gilles Barbier commanding the 1st Army, “famed for his politics and never his prowess”; Lt. General Jérémie De Michele of the Cavalry Division; Bg. General Gilbert Roulet of the Heavy Brigade; Colonel Jonathan Couturier of the Archduke Leopold Cuirassiers; Bg. General Sylvestre Laurens of the Dragoon Brigade; Colonel Edgar Dupuis of Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons; Colonel Martin Breguet of Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons; Bg. General Yvan Bellefeuille of the Guards Brigade; Colonel Ademar Gueguen of the Cardinal’s Guard; General Michel Masse commanding the 2nd Army; Lt. General Pierre-Paul Auclair of its 1st Division; Bg. General Xavier Cherbuliez of the 2nd Brigade of Foot; and Lt. Colonel Anton Drapeau, acting commander of the 53rd Fusiliers.

ArcticFox (player) — “So basically all of us on campaign should get ready — READY TO DIE”

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “Whoooooo — Let Me At Them”

The record

Letters from the front (June–July 1624)

Three game months passed on campaign before the guns spoke. The soldiers wrote home.

Pierre de Tête — “Letters from the Front: ‘June has been unpleasantly wet. I’m having trouble keeping my socks dry and my groom needs to clean out the horses’ feet three times a day. No action yet, but the Dragoons are every valiant and vigilent. / Your loving son, Pierre. P.S. How’s the family business doing?’”

Leon Mandelbrot — writes letters to Laurence Caillet. I miss her company and her cooking.

Narrator — “Mlle. Caillet has been writing you every day. Her letters rejuvenate you and bring you the courage needed to face battle. She misses a day in early July, and you spend the rest of the week fretting. But then her letters resume, more full of love and heartache than ever.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “I continue to write to Mille. Caillet, regailing her with stories, mostly made up, of the front lines, how boring they are, which privates blew themselves up with their own weapons, the hijinx of command. It helps alleviate the boredom of the front and gives a poor subaltern something to look forward to upon his return.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — “Jean-Luc doesn’t bother writing to Mlle Demachy, thinking instead on the 240 crowns he has already given her. … Tears fall onto his worn-out boots, washing a small area clean of horse excrement.”

Private Phillipe du Kaneda, meanwhile, confided in his diary in the fusiliers’ camp:

Phillipe du Kaneda — “Dear Diary, Today I had to marched around some more. Why? Because that incompetent Lieutenant D. Golém of the Royal Marines cannot see my true potential! … Now I am stuck here with the 53rd Fusiliers trying not to blow my own hand off with a these weapons they dare to call adequate for soldiering. The only saving grace in this whole mess is my superior officer Leon Mandelbrot. … I am happy he is by my side, leading whatever charge may come. For now they have us on defensive duty. God help me if I don’t go insane with boredom standing guard duty watching the same empty field, day-in day-out, miles from the front. What I wouldn’t give for some brandy right about now.”

On the morning of battle:

Jean-Luc Aveline — “Jean-Luc says the rosary and makes his peace with God as dawn breaks. The whinny of horses and bustle of soldiers brings his attention to the matter at hand.”

Pierre de Tête — “Today is the day, men! Today we ride for France!”

The battle (August 1624)

Narrator — “The 1st Army takes the field. Jean-Luc, Pierre, Godefroy, and Etienne stand among their number. While the 2nd and 3rd Armies stay behind to defend against rebel incursions, the 1st is so lead deep strikes into the enemy rear, to sow confusion and discord, and ultimately shatter the morale of the traitorous peasantry.”

Narrator — “General Gilles Barbier, famed for his politics and never his prowess, deploys his divisions.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1 (the worst possible command result)

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “CONFUSION AND DISCORD”

Narrator — “The cavalry division commander, Lt. General Jérémie De Michele, tries not to think about the stink of wine on the General’s breath as he commits his forces to a frontal assault on the enemy position.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “At the last moment, he calls for the bugler to signal a feint, and the horses dart from the field, carrying their riders to safety, but at some cost.”

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “What was this? I had finally committed myself mentally to the charge.”

Narrator — “The commander of the Heavy Brigade, Bg. General Gilbert Roulet, orders his men forward anyway.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “Colonel Jonathan Couturier, in command of the Archduke Leopold Cuirassiers, doesn’t even think twice before ordering his men forward for glory.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “The Cuirassiers are massacred, driven from the field in droves.”

Narrator — “Early reports were mistaken. As the smoke clears, it becomes obvious that some men still remain standing and able to fight. The result of the action for the regiment is Inconclusive.”

Here Private Jean-Luc Aveline’s fate was decided — see The reckoning below.

Narrator — “Just behind the Heavy Brigade, the Dragoon Brigade, led by Bg. General Sylvestre Laurens, comes charging. Bg. General Laurens is an old soldier, who’s gotten where he sits by keeping his mouth shut and doing his duty. If he recognizes the orders of his superiors for madness, he isn’t saying.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Colonel Edgar Dupuis leads his Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons forward, preparing to give the order to dismount and take the fight to the enemy once the time is right. It is beginning to seem the time may never be right. He takes another swig from his hip flask and tries to remember his adjutant’s name.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “Pierre, in command of 3rd Squadron, must decide in the moment whether to follow his commander’s confused orders, or take initiative on his own. Such is the prerogative of a Squadron Commander.”

Pierre de Tête — “Dragoons, seize the day!”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “For the 3rd Squadron, as well, the result of the action is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “Alongside Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons receive the same orders. But Colonel Martin Breguet is a cunning man of brilliant strategic mind. He demonstrates this by waiting to see what happens to GDM’s Dragoons before committing his forces.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “That hesitation proves decisive. Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons are set upon by enemy ambush, and driven from the field.”

Narrator — “As the Cavalry Division advances, the Guards Brigade moves in from the other side. Still a charge across an open field, as per the General’s orders, but the Guards, afoot, are able to move under light cover. Bg. General Yvan Bellefeuille is widely considered an idiot who can barely lace his breeches, but his adjutant, a Major from the King’s Musketeers, is considered a competent sort.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Bg. General Bellefeuille intends to ride up and down the line shouting encouragements to his sneaking men, but his adjutant hands him a sandwich. Only half of the line is revealed.”

Narrator — “The Cardinal’s Guard, commanded by Colonel Ademar Gueguen, a political appointee if there ever was one, follows the Brigadier’s lead. Colonel Gueguen can’t seem to help but shout back at his commander, much to his adjutant’s chagrin.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “The enemy quickly spot and isolate the Cardinal’s Guard. The regiment is completely crushed.”

By this point in the evening the 1st Army had cost three player characters their lives. The scene shifted to the defensive lines.

Narrator — “The 2nd Army watches from the fortifications as the 1st is almost completely wiped out. Stragglers flee back to the defensive line while the rebels stream in behind them, screaming incoherently.”

Jean-Luc Aveline (from the great beyond) — “You guys got this!”

Narrator — “General Michel Masse, standing proudly atop a hastily erected wooden command tower, raises a pristine white glove and signals to his men to prepare to receive an attack. They probably already knew that.”

Phillipe du Kaneda — Stares blankly for a moment at the field he is guarding that is suppose to be empty.

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The division commanders request the General’s orders. They are told: ‘En Garde!’”

Narrator — “The 1st Division, commanded by Lt. General Pierre-Paul Auclair, assorts itself into a textbook defense against a very non-textbook opponent.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The commander of the 2nd Brigade of Foot, Bg. General Xavier Cherbuliez, is well-known among military circles as a man who never misses a trick. He orders his regiments into two staggered lines.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “They don’t seem to know what that means.”

Narrator — “The 53rd Fusiliers have lost their brilliant Colonel to serve as General Masse’s adjutant. In his place, Lt. Colonel Anton Drapeau waves his arms around helplessly, struggling to direct the men to point their weapons in the proper direction.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Leon can do nought but watch as a cadre of rebels carry a huge wooden plank and throw it up and across the meager wall. The makeshift ramp crushes the skull of a young private on its descent. The regiment is completely overrun.” (The Narrator clarified at once: “that wasn’t Phillipe, to be clear. different private.”)

The fusiliers’ personal reckonings — one survival, one plank — follow below. With the 1st Division bleeding on the wall, the trap at last closed:

Narrator — “The 3rd and 4th Brigade of Foot rally together. The Royal Northern Highlands Brigade and Frontier Regiments swing in from the side. The valiant sacrifice of the 1st Division is sufficient to enable the 2nd Division and 3rd Army to perform a classic pincer maneuver. The rebel army is wiped from the surface of the Earth…but at what cost?”

Jean-Luc Aveline (from the great beyond) — “I think that went well”

The reckoning

Before the first personal roll of the night, the Narrator laid out the two ways a gentleman may tilt the odds — the moral machinery of every campaign to come:

Narrator — “If you choose poltroonery, you may add any number you feel necessary to the die roll needed for death. You then roll a d6 — on a 1 or 2, your cowardice escapes notice, but you miss the chance for plunder (promotion/despatches are rolled as normal). On a 3, 4, or 5, you are seen, and lose twice the modification you took in SP every month going forward until you are mentioned in despatches. On a 6, you are immediately dismissed from the regiment and may not apply to any regiment until you’ve been mentioned in despatches while in a Frontier Regiment. Your old regiment will never accept you again. You also suffer the above SP penalty.”

Narrator — “If you choose reckless bravery, you may subtract any number you wish from the die roll needed for death. You will use that modifier on the die rolls for despatches, promotion, and plunder as well.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — Private, Archduke Leopold Cuirassiers

Narrator — “Roll 2d6 for death against a 10. You have the options of poltroonery or reckless bravery if you wish to modify that roll. … You will, unmodified, roll for Mention against a 12, Promotion against a 7, and Plunder against an 11.”

Jean-Luc Aveline — “I choose reckless bravery modifier of 4.”

Narrator — “Very well. Roll 2d6 against a 6 for Death.”

🎲 Jean-Luc Aveline rolls 2d6 (#For France!) — 6 (Death target 6: dies)

Jean-Luc Aveline — “For Glory!”

Narrator — “Jean-Luc is one of the ones who does not return from the charge…”

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “Glory is a demanding mistress.”

Pierre de Tête — commanding 3rd Squadron, Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons

Narrator — “Pierre, your rolls will be Death: 12, Mention: 12, Promotion: 7, Plunder: 9. Choose whether to show poltroonery/reckless bravery.”

Pierre de Tête — “Reckless Bravery for 3!”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — 6 (Death target 9: survives)

Narrator — “At least one will return from battle this season.”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — 12 (Mention target 9: mentioned!)

Narrator — “You have been Mentioned in Despatches. News of your courage has been sent to Paris and all the city is abuzz. Roll a die.”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “You will receive +3 SP per month for the next 3 months, and then +1 SP per month for the rest of your life.”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — 6 (Promotion target 4: qualifies)

Narrator — “Although you qualify for a promotion, there are no openings in your Regiment. You would have been Mentioned in Despatches instead, but you were already. You get a pat on the back.”

🎲 Pierre de Tête rolls 2d6 — 10 (Plunder target 6: success) · then 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “You manage to snag a purse containing 50 Crowns from a dead peasant leader.”

Pierre de Tête — “I raise his dead hand and also use it to pat myself on the back”

Godefroy de Beaufosse — Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons

Narrator — “Your rolls will be Death: 8, Mention: 10, Promotion: 6, Plunder: 11. Decide between poltroonery/RB now.”

Godefroy de Beaufosse — “Perhaps reckless bravery has its rewards… RB for 2”

🎲 Godefroy de Beaufosse rolls 2d6 — 8 (Death target 6: dies)

Narrator — “Godefroy is pulled down from his mount and torn to shreds by bloodthirsty peasants.”

harry (player) — “Blerg!”

Jean-Luc Aveline (from the great beyond) — “Godefroy and Jean-Luc share a drink in the great beyond”

Etienne de Mercoeur — Cardinal’s Guard

Narrator — “Your rolls will be Death: 9, Mention: 7, Promotion: 5. Plunder is not possible.”

Etienne took no modifier — and needed no help.

🎲 Etienne de Mercoeur rolls 2d6 — 11 (Death target 9: dies)

Narrator — “Etienne is set upon, his heavy armor no defence against dozens of stabs from rusty farm implements.”

Etienne de Mercoeur — “Mon diiieuuuuuuuuuuuuu X_x”

Jean-Luc Aveline (from the great beyond) — “You are also welcome at my table”

Etienne de Mercoeur — “Oh dear. Well, here’s to your… afterlife.”

Leon Mandelbrot — Subaltern, 53rd Fusiliers

Narrator — “Leon, your unmodified rolls will be Death: 8, Mention: 11, Promotion: 6. No chance for Plunder.”

Leon, alone that night, took neither bravery nor poltroonery.

🎲 Leon Mandelbrot rolls 2d6 — 6 (Death target 8: survives)

Leon Mandelbrot — “I LIVE”

🎲 Leon Mandelbrot rolls 2d6 — 9 (Mention target 11: no mention) 🎲 Leon Mandelbrot rolls 2d6 — 8 (Promotion target 6: qualifies)

Narrator — “You might not get it. one of the captains has to die. let’s find out”

🎲 Narrator rolls 2d6 — 8 (a captain has died)

Narrator — “Yep you are promoted to Captain”

🎲 Narrator rolls 2d6, five times — 10, 4, 5, 11, 8

Narrator — “You’ll be in charge of C Company”

Pierre de Tête (DancingHorse, at the table) — “that means 4 captains died in the fight”

Phillipe du Kaneda — Private, 53rd Fusiliers

Narrator — “Your unmodified rolls are Death: 7, Mention: 11, Promotion: 6. No plunder.”

Phillipe du Kaneda — “NO! I have guarded the field for too long to have some ruffians take it from me!”

🎲 Phillipe du Kaneda rolls 2d6 — 9 (Death target 7: dies)

Phillipe du Kaneda — “I take three of them with me”

Narrator — “Phillipe gets crushed by another big wooden plank, just as the old gypsy woman predicted.”

Phillipe du Kaneda — “After the battle has subsided, when not one is around, Phillipe’s corpse appears to start to stream off a gold like dust…”

Honors, casualties, and the ledger

Narrator — “The campaign has ended.”

Four player characters were dead in a single night — Jean-Luc Aveline, Godefroy de Beaufosse, Etienne de Mercoeur, and Phillipe du Kaneda — the bloodiest single session of the entire game. Among the NPCs:

Narrator — “The commander of the Heavy Brigade, Bg. General Gilbert Roulet, is counted among the dead.”

Four captains of the 53rd Fusiliers also fell, opening Leon Mandelbrot’s path to a captaincy — a promotion with strings attached:

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “In order to keep your captaincy you are required to purchase a horse. This will cost 100 crowns, plus a monthly cost of 3 in feed and 2 to pay the groom”

Leon Mandelbrot — “I purchased a horse and have named him Joyeux”

Guy de Cavoye — “And they say chivalry is dead.”

Pierre de Tête came home mentioned in despatches, 50 crowns richer, and solvent: “I managed to free myself of 250 crowns of debt during the summer campaign.” He promptly announced in the streets: “Gentleman, your hero, recently returned from a successful campaign, will be hosting at the Frog and Peach the third week of this month. All gentleman are invited to attend as carousing is covered by my battle plunder!” — to which the new-minted Captain Mandelbrot replied, “I would love to join you, and share my own stories that got me a promotion and got many of my fellow fusilier’s killed!”

Aftermath in the streets of Paris

The survivors and the newly rolled walked among the gossip. Leon could not stop talking about the plank:

Leon Mandelbrot — “To those going to war, beware of wooden planks!”

Jules Lavelle — “What, did one fall on your foot when you were building fortifications?”

Jules Lavelle — “The other officers of the Dragoon Guard are all a-talk about the failures of their fellow dragoons on campaign. Was it really so dreadful as all that? I should hate to be associated with disappointments.”

Guy de Cavoye — “From what I hear, blame it on the cavalry. Slipshod performance from what should have been a shining example of French military prowess.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “Oh their attack was a tremendous debacle, though there were some notes of bravery”

A muscular newcomer named Clemens Charron — harry’s next character — wandered in knowing nothing:

Jules Lavelle — “All this despondency. It was a great victory! The rebels were crushed!”

Clemens Charron — “Wait what … There was a rebellion?”

Clemens Charron — “I dunno, take a man’s salt and you’ve taken so much from him - the flavor in his food, the sweat off his back, and one of the few luxuries in his cupboard.”

Jules Lavelle — “I’m sorry? Are we affording mere peasants luxuries now? They should be grateful for whatever they can contribute to the greatness of France.”

Clemens Charron — “My good sir, peasants are the backbone of France! I understand the reluctance to spend your valuable time among those most fragrant, garrulous, and course, but all men have their pleasures.”

Jules Lavelle — “Villains are as villeins do, bear in mind.”

Clemens Charron — “Perhaps a strong drink is in order?”

Jules Lavelle — “As always!”

And WillWorker’s next character announced himself with a mission statement:

Hercule Bonaparte — “A frail youth walks down the street. Unlike his peers, the cane he uses is more than just a fashion accessory. It is likely he could move without it, but clear its support gives him comfort. Despite his delicate appearance, his eyes gleam with the cold steel edge of a military mind hungry to be tested. Could this young man be what France needs to prevent the Summer of 1624 for happening ever again?”

The next morning the Fall volunteer rolls went out — Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons, the Queen’s Own Carabiniers, and the Royal Marines all volunteered on 6s (“Bye Curtis,” “Bye Pierre,” “Bye Alphonse,” said the Narrator) — though Pierre de Tête was plucked from the rolls when the Dragoon Brigade named him Brigade Major, while Jules Lavelle was chosen Brigade Major of the Horse Guards. The Croquant story itself ended some weeks later, on a scaffold in Paris:

Narrator — “A crowd gathers in the square before city hall. The officers of the Cardinal’s Guard arrived early in the morning to oversee the workers assembling the gallows. As the morning mists steam off of the cobblestones, the rebel leaders Donat and Barran are marched up onto the platform where they will meet their demise.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “Their rampage killed my friends Phillipe du Kaneda and Jean-Luc Aveline!”

Narrator — “He finishes and turns away, disappearing from view. Moments later, the secretary nods to the Escort of the Cardinal’s Guard. The trapdoors are sprung. The rebel leaders fall, and rise to their final reward. The safety of France is ensured for one more day.”

Table talk

ArcticFox — “So what are our chances of surviving realistically?” · Jules Lavelle (GM) — “For privates and subalterns on defensive missions, it’s low”

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “But you can always try to save yourself with poltroonery” · WillWorker — “You spelled pragmatism wrong.”

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “Discretion is the better part of valor. Cowardice is the better part of discretion”

etirflita — “Is it better to roll fewer times or more?” · Jules Lavelle (GM) — “More” · etirflita — “I, uh, take back all the things I’ve been saying about @Dice Golem”

ArcticFox — “BE BRAVE DONT THINK — GLORY” · harry — “POL THAT TROON” (moments before both of their characters’ death rolls)

ArcticFox — “KANEDAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAAA”

etirflita — “I assume if you borrow money, a good way to avoid paying it back is to die?” · Narrator — “that is the best way”

DancingHorse — “I’ll bring flowers to your gravestone every month” · harry — “just bring a nice steak every so often”

Fall 1624 — “My Life for France!”: The Siege of the Valtellina Forts

September–November 1624 · resolved at the table 26–27 September 2020 · Mission: Siege · Theater: the Valtellina, against the Spanish garrisons of the Alpine valley

The situation

The great army that crushed the Croquants had barely limped home when the fall levies went out. This was to be the first volunteer campaign of the game — no royal summons, only the luck of the regimental dice:

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “For the Fall season, rolling to see if any regiments or battalions are volunteered for duty. … they go on a 6. … this can be modified by influence, if desired”

Two and a half weeks later at the table — with the game clock run forward to November 1624 — the bill came due:

Narrator — “Preparing the volunteer campaign’s resolution.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Curtis Sinnoch (player) — “awwww yisssssss”

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiment has been assigned to a Siege mission. They will attempt to capture the forts of Valtellina.”

With the assignment the Narrator posted an old engraving of a star-fort above the valley — Forte de Fuentes, “Parte verso Valtelino”:

The stakes of that valley were only spelled out at the table much later, when the war returned there in 1626 — but the Narrator’s explanation serves as well for 1624:

Narrator (at the return to the Valtellina, 1626) — “Although the people of Valtellina themselves are Protestant, the Swiss Grison League has aligned itself with France to liberate the region from the hands of the Spanish and Papal armies occupying the valley. With Venice and Savoy also providing support, this is France’s best chance to drive a wedge between the Habsburgs and maintain its strategic position in Europe – a cause worth cooperating with a few Protestants over.”

The order of battle

The volunteer dice of early September had picked their victims on 6s: the Queen’s Own Carabiniers (“Bye @Curtis Sinnoch,” said the Narrator), Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons (“Bye @DancingHorse”), the 1st Squadron of Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons, and the 3rd Battalion of the Royal Marines (“Bye @Anonymouse”).

Narrator — “Barring exerting Influence on their commanders, @Curtis Sinnoch , @DancingHorse , and @Anonymouse will be campaigning in the Fall season”

Pierre de Tête escaped the rolls at once — “Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons. @DancingHorse has been chosen for the position [of Dragoon Brigade Major]. … As such, he has been removed from volunteering for campaign.” Jules Lavelle was likewise named Brigade Major of the Horse Guards, Léonce Masse of the Picardy Musketeers was chosen for the First Brigade “by virtue of being related to General Masse,” and Gaël Houdin of the 53rd Fusiliers took the 2nd Brigade.

etirflita (player) — “This is for the entire season, n’est-ce pas?”

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “The people being volunteered are gone until December”

Curtis Sinnoch, a Captain of the Queen’s Own Carabiniers, refused to march as a mere line officer:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Well, Looks like I shall be heading into battle this Fall! I might as well use my tactical acumen and insight where it can best serve France! I shall see if the Colonel will have my as a Regimental Adjutant! (5+ roll needed, utilizing two class 3 favors to give myself a +2 to the roll, along with my SL giving me a +2 Bonus. I am looking at a 1 or higher.)”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6+4 — (3)+4 = 7 (adjutancy secured)

Curtis Sinnoch — “For King and Country!”

Alphonse Devereux, Captain in the Royal Marines, took the news with grace:

Alphonse Devereux — “I would love to join you both, but I must take my turn at war.”

And Guy de Cavoye — a bastard son newly arrived in Paris, a subaltern in the Royal Foot Guards for exactly one week — volunteered himself:

Guy de Cavoye — “After a week of duty I break the news to my commander that I will not be able to report for duty next month as I, @etirflita, am volunteering to fight for my country!”

Narrator — “Col. Bouchard looks at @etirflita as though he has lost his mind.”

Guy de Cavoye — “One hopes he can find it on the battlefield..”

From the front, in September, one letter home survives:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah, my dearest Adelaide, how I miss you so. It is difficult on the fronts, but I know we shall be victorious in this campaign! Then I shall return, and we can be together once again!”

When the siege lines were drawn, the Narrator sorted the volunteers:

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch , @etirflita , and @Anonymouse are fighting. Curtis serves under his regiment. Guy and Alphonse have been placed into the chain of command of the Frontier Regiment itself.”

Character Rank / post Serving with
Curtis Sinnoch Captain & Regimental Adjutant Queen’s Own Carabiniers, under Col. Denis Nee
Guy de Cavoye Subaltern (Royal Foot Guards), volunteer chain of command of the Frontier Regiment, under Col. Rémy Aymard
Alphonse Devereux Captain, Royal Marines 3rd Battalion chain of command of the Frontier Regiment

The two NPC colonels got their vignettes in a single breath:

Narrator — “The two regiments operate independently, with little communication. Col. Denis Nee, commanding the Queen’s Own Carabiniers, is a leader of great foresight and reliability. He convinces the Frontier Regiment’s commander, Col. Rémy Aymard, to conduct a frontal attack to try to break the siege, while the Q.O.C. scale the walls unnoticed.”

The record

The Frontier Regiment at the gates

Narrator — “The defenses have been battered by weeks of blockade and cannonade, but the walls remain intact. The Frontier Regiment rushes toward the gates under heavy fire. ‘My life for France!’ screams Col. Aymard.”

Narrator — “The frazzled, exhausted Spanish defenders may not understand the shout, but they know too well its meaning.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3 (Col. Aymard’s command)

Narrator — “Col. Aymard’s orders are solid, but no less solid than the gates themselves. The attackers are repelled, but in good order, and return to their lines mostly intact. The outcome of the Frontier Regiment’s role in the siege is Inconclusive.”

Guy de Cavoye’s personal rolls followed at once — see The reckoning below.

The Queen’s Own Carabiniers on the far wall

Narrator — “As the fighting against the gate reaches its peak, around the far side of the fort, Col. Nee gives the order for his men to deploy grapnels and begin their ascent.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 5 (Col. Nee’s command)

Narrator — “The men throw with all their might, but there are few defenses at this part of the wall, and therefore little purchase. A few defenders throw rocks, but there aren’t enough targets on the wall for it to be very dangerous. After thirty minutes of fruitless effort, Col. Nee calls off the attack.”

Narrator — “The Queen’s Own Carabiniers’ role in the siege is Inconclusive.”

Two assaults, two shrugs from the dice. The forts did fall in the end — the December Gazette de France ran the campaign’s epitaph in its Military News column, the most honest sentence it ever printed:

Gazette de France, Novembre 1624 — “News from the front: the Frontier Regiment was triumphant at Valtellina, once the defenders ran out of food.”

The reckoning

Guy de Cavoye — Subaltern, with the Frontier Regiment

Guy de Cavoye — “2d6, n’est-ce pas?”

Narrator — “Yes”

Narrator — “You have been spared!”

Honors, plunder, and the road home

The ledger of the fall: no player character dead, one Q.O.C. captain fallen, Alphonse Devereux Mentioned in Despatches with two hundred crowns of stolen heraldry in his baggage, and nothing at all for the men at the gates. Back in the streets of Paris, when Jules Lavelle asked aloud whether anyone might loan him 240 crowns:

Guy de Cavoye — “Alas, the fighting was not as lucrative as I might have hoped.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah, to be back in the grand city of Paris, with my pockets flush with cash after several months not spending anything on that damn campaign! I’m sure I was this close to a promotion or other notice, but c’est la vie! To be home once more, and to LIVE!”

Alphonse Devereux — “Hear, hear!”

Alphonse Devereux — “@Curtis Sinnoch, I would love to catch up with you after this campaign to trade tales. Would you care to host at Hunter’s this month?”

There was no rest in it. Within the same sitting the Narrator was already rolling for the Winter Campaign — “Royal Foot Guards are going on campaign. Bye @etirflita” … “53rd Fusiliers are going. Bye @ArcticFox and @laiv.wire” — and Guy, twice a volunteer now, saluted:

Guy de Cavoye — “At least I shall be paid my regiment’s salary this time!”

Winter 1624–25 — The Storming of Blavet

December 1624 – February 1625 · resolved at the table 17–18 October 2020 · Mission: Siege (amphibious assault) · Theater: Blavet harbor, Brittany

The situation

The Huguenot rebellion had put a fleet to sea, and the winter’s work began on the water. When the mission die came up 1 — “This is a Siege,” the Narrator announced — he set the scene:

Narrator — “After a decisive naval engagement between the Royal Fleet commanded by the Duke of Nevers and the Huguenot Navy under the Duke of Soubise, the rebels found their backs broken by the brilliant maneuvers of Nevers, who said ‘Never!’ to the chances of Huguenot independence.”

Narrator — “The Huguenots retreat into the fort in Blavet harbor. The Duke of Vendôme is assigned to conduct an amphibious landing and take the fort.”

Word of the deployment had gone out weeks earlier, back in November. The Royal Foot Guards and the 53rd Fusiliers volunteered on 6s, and the men marked for the boats took the news each in his own key:

Guy de Cavoye — “At least I shall be paid my regiment’s salary this time!”

Henri Toulouse — “Well, why not. My luck has been total shite, so my expectations can only be met, not disappointed.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “We’ll be fine Henri! … I’ll be helping in the direction from atop my horse! … Oh wait, revieweing the rosters, you’re not in my company! … Best of luck then!”

Henri Toulouse — “One small mercy, I suppose.”

Leon Mandelbrot — “In fairness, being in my company did not help poor Phillipe”

The order of battle

In the January before the sailing, two gentlemen bought their way to field rank — Curtis Sinnoch purchasing a majority in the King’s Musketeers (“Congratulations to the new junior Major in the King’s Musketeers!” — the Narrator), and Hercule Bonaparte at last winning entry to the Crown Prince Cuirassiers:

Hercule Bonaparte — “At last, I can prove my mettle.”

Guy de Cavoye — “We have some Major spenders here!”

Both new majors volunteered their own battalions for the winter campaign. On the eve of departure the streets emptied of soldiers:

Guy de Cavoye — “I once again feel the love of France burning in my loins!”

Jules Lavelle — “Which way to the camp followers, then, sir?”

Hercule Bonaparte — “Hercule sees the stream of the young men of France heading out to campaign. The question of Jules Lavelle regarding his service to France echoing in his ear. The fame that such service could garner echoing even louder. ‘I have my commission now,’ he mutters to himself, ‘and one can only get so far on carousing and politicking… no sense leaving all the glory to these lot.’ He writes two letters. The first, instructions to his staff regarding upkeep of the estate while he is away. The second, a brief note to Alida explaining his absence and that he will keep the calm understanding of her company within his heart. A moment of regretful emotion crosses his face before returning to its normal steel-eyed gaze. He dons his uniform, gives himself a steady once over in the full length mirror, and heads out to war.”

Jules Lavelle — “Knee-deep in prostitutes, Jules momentarily realizes he forgot to tell Eugenie where he’d be this month. Ah, well. C’est la guerre, c’est l’amour.”

Guy de Cavoye — “It feels good to have so many of my countrymen join me in this campaign!”

Seven players answered the muster call — but the staff officers were promptly sent back to their desks:

Narrator — “Correction. Jules Lavelle is serving as brigade major and not allowed to volunteer. His month is wasted” — “Same goes for DancingHorse [Pierre de Tête].”

Narrator — “etirflita [Guy de Cavoye] campaigns with the Royal Foot Guards. Curtis Sinnoch has volunteered his battalion, operating under the Frontier Regiment. WillWorker [Hercule Bonaparte] has volunteered his battalion, operating under the Frontier Regiment. ArcticFox [Leon Mandelbrot] and laiv.wire [Henri Toulouse] campaign with the 53rd Fusiliers.”

Character Rank / post Unit
Guy de Cavoye Subaltern Royal Foot Guards (in reserve)
Leon Mandelbrot Captain, C Company 53rd Fusiliers
Henri Toulouse Subaltern, D Company 53rd Fusiliers
Curtis Sinnoch Major, commanding 3rd Battalion King’s Musketeers, under the Frontier Regiment
Hercule Bonaparte Major, commanding 3rd Squadron Crown Prince Cuirassiers, under the Frontier Regiment
Jules Lavelle Brigade Major staff — barred from volunteering
Pierre de Tête Brigade Major staff — barred from volunteering

Above them all stood the Duke of Vendôme, in overall command of the landing; Colonel Sébastien Besson of the 53rd Fusiliers; and Colonel Bouchard of the Royal Foot Guards.

ArcticFox (player) — “WE’RE ALL GONNA DIE”

The record

The landing (February 1625)

Narrator — “Vendôme commands the 53rd and his own battalions to storm the fort directly. The Royal Foot Guards will wait in reserve and move to take advantage of any sudden gaps in the defenses.”

Guy de Cavoye — “Watch for planks!”

ArcticFox (player) — (reading the orders back) “Vendôme commands the 53rd and his own battalions to storm the fort directly. … Fuuuck”

Henri Toulouse — “Ouais…”

Henri Toulouse — “\<boredly waving saber> Vive le France…”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for Vendôme’s command — 1

Narrator — “Vendôme’s orders are muddled and confused. The landing boats are tossed into disarray.”

ArcticFox (player) — “Oh thats bad”

Narrator — “WillWorker, roll 1d6 for your battle result. You will have a -1 modifier to your MA due to your commander’s incompetence.” (and the same to Curtis Sinnoch)

The King’s Musketeers’ 3rd Battalion went in first:

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Curtis Sinnoch, you manage to gather your battalion together and attempt an attack on the gate. Unfortunately, a sortie emerges, and your men are forced into cover under a withering hail of gunfire. The result of your battle is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “You gain +1 MA for salvaging a stalemate from the jaws of disaster.”

Then the fusiliers:

Narrator — “Sébastien Besson, Colonel of the 53rd Fusiliers, has seen many battles in his time. He manages to keep his boats together and land on the beach as one regiment.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for the 53rd — 5

Narrator — “Col. Besson takes charge of the landing, and his regiment steps out onto the beach before the imposing fortress in good battle order. The 1st Battalion provides cover while the 2nd & 3rd advance up the beach. They harass the flanks of the enemy sorties, and fully establish a beachhead!”

Here Captain Leon Mandelbrot’s and Subaltern Henri Toulouse’s fates were decided — see The reckoning below. With the beachhead won, the reserve was committed:

Narrator — “With the establishment of a beachhead, Col. Bouchard of the Royal Foot Guards orders his men to charge forward to reinforce the beleaguered 53rd.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for the Royal Foot Guards — 6

Narrator — “The enemy attempts to pelt the 53rd with hand grenades, but the Royal Foot Guards toss them right back with their handy lacrosse sticks.”

Narrator — “A successful storming!”

It was in this storm of returned grenades that Subaltern Guy de Cavoye met his end — see below. Last of all came the Crown Prince Cuirassiers, horsemen put into rowboats:

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 1

WillWorker (player) — “oof”

Narrator — “Hercule’s battalion, without horses, is utterly unprepared for the intensity of fighting amphibiously. They suffer horrible losses as they find themselves repelled back into the sea.”

And when the last man’s dice had fallen, the Narrator pronounced the verdict of the day:

Narrator — “The fighting outside the fortress is madness. Men pour from boats and the fort, scream, and die. Great struggles are waged over bare feet of land between the sea and fortress walls. Although the armies of France are valiant, they are unable to make real progress in seizing the fortress. They hold position throughout the day, but as night falls, each battalion slips back to its boats and retreats from the harbor. The Huguenots are victorious.

Duke Soubise names himself ‘Admiral of the Protestant Church.’”

The reckoning

Leon Mandelbrot — Captain, C Company, 53rd Fusiliers

Narrator — “ArcticFox, your rolls are: Death 9, Mention 9, Promotion 7, Crowns 7 … Poltroonery/Reckless Bravery?” (The arithmetic, as the Narrator explained it: “for a Siege 2, the base is Death 8, Mention 9, Promotion 7, Crowns 8 — as a Captain you get +1 to Death and -1 to Crowns.”)

ArcticFox (player) — “Neither … Wait … Then Reckless bravery at TWO”

Narrator — “Death 6 … er … 7”

Hercule Bonaparte — “catching his breath when the battle was over It appears war is just like love. The newness of the first time carries with it so much… it can overwhelm the sense. But now I know.”

Honors, casualties, and the ledger

Two player characters died in the surf and smoke at Blavet — Leon Mandelbrot, drowned under his own landing plank, and Guy de Cavoye, exploded by a grenade his comrades failed to lacrosse away. Henri Toulouse came home a Captain (stepping, in all likelihood, into his dead comrade’s slot); Curtis Sinnoch was mentioned in despatches (+1 SP per month for life) and 1,000 crowns richer for his boathouse discovery; Hercule Bonaparte was mentioned in despatches after his fighting retreat.

The NPC officers fared little better. In the Royal Foot Guards the Lieutenant-Colonel and three captains were killed (rolls of 9, 8, 9, and 11 against their death numbers); Curtis’s own battalion of the King’s Musketeers lost no officers; the Crown Prince Cuirassiers lost a captain —

Narrator — “Couldn’t lead him out of that one, WillWorker....”

WillWorker (player) — “That man was an idiot. If I could, I would have prompted his horse to his position.”

— and the 53rd Fusiliers lost their Lieutenant-Colonel, a Major, and Captain D. Except:

Narrator — “oh never mind … Cap D was Leon”

laiv.wire (player) — “Double death”

ArcticFox (player) — “NOOOOOOO … Wait … Does that mean I come back to life?”

Narrator — “you come back to life. your lungs are still full of water. you drown again immediately”

ArcticFox (player) — “Worth”

In the promotion rolls that followed, a Major and a Captain of the Royal Foot Guards and a Captain of the 53rd Fusiliers were advanced. Then came the spring musters: the Dragoon Guards were volunteered (“As Brigade Major, Jules is exempted from service,” the Narrator noted), and both Curtis Sinnoch and Hercule Bonaparte chose to lead their battalions out yet again — toward Nantes, and the Huguenot army now marching in triumph from Blavet.

Aftermath in Paris (March 1625)

The defeat was too shameful to print:

Narrator (in the Gazette de France) — “By Order of the Cardinal there is to be no Gazette published this month. A bounty of 10,000 crowns has been offered for the head of the Duke of Soubise.”

Clemens Charron — “Is there a bounty for any other part of the man?”

In the streets, the survivors and the newly arrived took stock:

Lucien Vaillancourt — “War is hell, boys. Those who come back come back as changed men. Those who do not come back come back as changed men.”

Jules Lavelle — “You think that’s hell? Try running errands for a geriatric brigadier-general! … I’d take the war any day....”

Clemens Charron — “It would seem that hell is, most accurately, others.”

And the man with the boathouse chest turned his plunder to hospitality:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Fine Gentleman! To be back upon the streets, as the brutal chill of winter starts to leave, so does my heart once warm! Life! To Life! All men of good standing and intentions are invited to join me at Hunter’s this month! The last of the month! The first round is on me!”

Table talk

Narrator — “This is a Siege.” · ArcticFox — “WE’RE ALL GONNA DIE”

❦

Spring 1625 — The Fall of Nantes

March – May 1625 · resolved at the table 8 November 2020 · Mission: Defense · Theater: Nantes, on the Loire

The situation

The winter had ended in humiliation at Blavet, and the spring began with the bill coming due. When the mission die came up 5, the Narrator declared it:

Narrator — “The Spring campaign is a Defense mission.”

Narrator — “The Huguenots, fresh from their victory at Blavet, have raised a large army. Their navy has already set sail, and the army now travels south, seeking to link up with the larger part of the Huguenot force at La Rochelle.”

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiments have been deployed to Nantes to set up a defensive line. Success here will slow or halt the Huguenot advance. But the Protestants outnumber the Catholics three to one. Their approaching flags seem to stretch forever.”

Only two gentlemen of the table stood in that line — the same two majors who had waded ashore at Blavet and lived. Curtis Sinnoch’s farewell to the capital was, as ever, delivered at full voice:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah, the feel of the wind! The air itself! It stirs me and my men to action! Farewell, Paris, for @Curtis Sinnoch marches to war!”

The order of battle

The battalion volunteer rolls had gone out back in February. Among the units drawn for the front, two were no accident of the dice:

Narrator — “KM3 is the choice of @Curtis Sinnoch” · “CPC3 is the choice of @WillWorker”

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch and @WillWorker have volunteered on campaign.”

Narrator — “Each leads his battalion under the Frontier Regiment Colonel, who is Colonel Christian Solé this season.”

Character Rank / post Unit
Curtis Sinnoch Major, commanding 3rd Battalion King’s Musketeers, under the Frontier Regiment
Hercule Bonaparte Major, commanding 3rd Squadron Crown Prince Cuirassiers, under the Frontier Regiment

Above them: Colonel Christian Solé of the Frontier Regiment, whose one recorded vignette is the campaign’s whole tragedy in miniature —

Narrator — “Colonel Solé, clear-eyed and hale, delivers a speech in which he declares that the enemy may be numerous, but quality is on the side of the French Army.”

Opposite them: the entire Huguenot field army, three men to every one of the King’s.

The record

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for Colonel Solé’s command — 2

Narrator — “Col. Solé battle result: 5”

A battle result of 5 — on a scale where low numbers are glory and high numbers are ruin — and the Narrator supplied the prose to match:

Narrator — “Unfortunately, the valorous part of the French Army is back in Paris. Mid-speech, Solé notices the Huguenots have begun their charge. He spurs his horse…away from the battlefield, leaving his adjutant to give orders.”

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch and @WillWorker, roll to determine your battle results as your positions are overrun by hordes of screaming Protestants.”

The King’s Musketeers hold the line

Curtis Sinnoch (player) — “(1d6, right?)”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 — 6 · Narrator — “Battle result: 2”

Narrator — “Curtis leads his men in a desperate counter action. It seems that the quality of the King’s Musketeers really does shine through, as the attackers melt away when faced with the fury of their musket fire.”

Narrator — “Curtis, your rolls are Death 9, Despatches 9, Promotion 7, Crowns 6”

Six minutes later the Narrator checked his arithmetic and dimmed the glory by one notch:

Narrator — “Correction. You barely hold off the attackers. Your actual result is a 3.”

Narrator — “Death 11, Despatches 11, Promotion 9, Crowns 9”

The Cuirassiers charge

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 5 · Narrator — “Battle Result: 2”

Narrator — “The Cuirassiers charge over the battlefield, running roughshod over the underequipped and unprepared Protestant militia.”

Narrator — “Death 10, Despatches 10, Promotion 8, Crowns 6”

The verdict

Two majors had beaten their colonel’s battle result by two and three points respectively — and it was not enough. When both men’s personal fates had been rolled out (see The reckoning), the Narrator pronounced the day:

Narrator — “Despite great showings of valor from volunteer majors, the bulk of the Frontier Regiments is unable to resist the unrelenting assault from the Huguenots. Nantes is overrun, and Colonel Solé executed for his poltroonery.”

The colonel who fled his own speech was thus the campaign’s only recorded officer fatality — shot not by the enemy but by the King’s justice.

The reckoning

Curtis Sinnoch — Major, 3rd Battalion, King’s Musketeers

Against targets of Death 11, Despatches 11, Promotion 9, Crowns 9, the hero of the Blavet boathouse played it straight for once:

Curtis Sinnoch — “I will not be attempting any reckless bravery”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Rolling for Death!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 5 (Death target 11: survives) · Narrator — “Alive!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 7 (Mention target 11: fails) · Narrator — “Unknown!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 8 (Promotion) · Narrator — “Promoted!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 5 (Crowns target 9: fails)

Narrator — “There are no open positions. You have been Mentioned in Despatches. Roll 1d6”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “+2 SP for the next 3 months, then +1 for life” — “er” — “+4” — “No loot”

Narrator — “Also, Curtis, for outperforming your commanding officer in the field, you gain +1 MA permanently.”

And then the sentence every ambitious officer waits for:

Narrator — “As this is your second time being mentioned in despatches, the King has caught wind of your talents.”

Narrator — “You may roll 1d6 to receive a Title on a roll of 6. This can be modified by Influence over the King, if you can muster it.”

Narrator — “Actually, due to your high SL, you only need a roll of 5.”

Curtis Sinnoch (player) — “king needs a Class 9 favor, right?”

Narrator — “Yes”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The King is about to sign the decree, but is distracted by his mistress. Maybe next time…”

Hercule Bonaparte — Major, 3rd Squadron, Crown Prince Cuirassiers

Against targets of Death 10, Despatches 10, Promotion 8, Crowns 6 — no reckless bravery this time, the memory of Blavet’s surf perhaps still fresh:

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 2d6 (#death) — 6 (Death target 10: survives)

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 2d6 (#despatches) — 7 (fails)

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 2d6 (#promo) — 7 (fails)

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 2d6 (#crowns) — 5 (fails)

Four flat failures after the death roll — until the GM checked the modifiers:

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “Sorry, should have been another -1 on Mentions/Promotion” — “So you actually do qualify for promotion!” — “As no positions are open, please roll a die for Despatches.”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “enjoy +1 SP/month for the rest of your life. also +1 MA”

Narrator — “The King is informed of your outstanding service to France.”

Narrator — “Roll 1d6 to see if you receive a Title. You would need a 6.”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 6 — a natural six, met at the table with a row of 😮 and 📜

Narrator — “Hercule Bonaparte has received the title of Knight!”

Narrator — “+10 SP next month”

Narrator — “Your SL is instantly raised to 10.”

WillWorker (player) — “That’s SIR Herule Bonaparte”

Hercule Bonaparte — “Hercule faces his knighthood with the same steel-eyed stone face he has become known for. As the King presents the new knight to the Court, the exceptionally observant note a slight upward curl at the corners of his mouth”

Minutes after the roll, the Narrator inaugurated the campaign’s #peerage channel — its very first post, Hercule’s patent of knighthood, saluted with six rounds of applause. The mechanics of the miracle were clarified afterward: the title chance came not from loot but from beating the fleeing Colonel Solé’s battle result by three full points — a 2 against a 5.

Honors, casualties, and the ledger

No player character so much as bled at Nantes — the butcher’s bill fell on the rank and file of the Frontier Regiments and on Colonel Christian Solé, executed for his poltroonery. Curtis Sinnoch came home with his second Mention in Despatches (+4 SP for three months, then +1 for life), a permanent +1 MA for outperforming his commanding officer, and a title roll spoiled by the royal mistress. Hercule Bonaparte came home a Knight — Mentioned in Despatches (+1 SP per month for life), +1 MA, +10 SP, and his Social Level vaulted instantly to 10. The city itself was the campaign’s casualty: Nantes belonged to the Huguenots, and the road to La Rochelle lay open.

There was no pause to mourn. That same evening the Minister of War drew up the summer’s lists:

Narrator — “The Minister of War decides who to draw up for the Summer Campaign.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Everyone except the 4th Foot Brigade is going on campaign.”

Narrator — “That means everyone, since Pain Loafmann is the only one without a regiment and he is being volunteered.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for brigade assignments — 6

Narrator — “Field Operations for everybody!”

ArcticFox (player) — “YAY!” — “WE ALL GET TO DIE!”

Aftermath: Sir Bonaparte at Hunter’s

The new knight could not collect his +10 SP until he returned to Paris — “You’ll get it the month you come back,” the Narrator ruled — and so the knighthood’s true coronation came months later, in September, at the door of Hunter’s:

Hercule Bonaparte — “For the first time, Hercule enters Hunter’s under the clout of his own name, his presence no longer requiring the grace of others. Tonight’s plans: a quiet meal at a table for two with the beauty Alida. And while their date is clearly a private affair, none mistake that she has dressed to capture the eyes of the room”

Narrator — “The maître d’ scrambles over, waving at the sommelier. ‘Sir Bonaparte and the lustrous Mlle. Demachy,’ he says, bowing deeply. ‘May Hunter’s present this finest bottle of 1599 Bordeaux, with our compliments to the hero!’”

Table talk

WillWorker — “Was the knighthood roll for succeeding on Crowns?” · Jules Lavelle (GM) — “The knighthood roll was for getting a battle result 3 higher than your commander … You didn’t succeed on crowns, you needed a 6 and got a 5” · WillWorker — “Right. I got confused. … Thought I had to roll under.”

WillWorker — “Mechanically, the knighthood makes me a noble now, right?” · Jules Lavelle (GM) — “Yep … For RP purposes higher nobles won’t really consider you a noble until one more rank … But mechanically you are a noble” · WillWorker — “Oh, I figure there is going to be some nouveau riche stuff going on. But I figured the difference between being considered a commoner or nobility will be the difference between iodine or salt in the wound.”

WillWorker — “I guess this means I don’t get to take advantage of the +10SP for being knighted....” · Narrator — “You’ll get it the month you come back” · DancingHorse — “@WillWorker you just need to live long enough!”

etirflita (on Pain Loafmann’s pending sentence, right after the fall of Nantes) — “So.. Pain doesn’t start his sentence until next month, correct?” · Narrator — “Correct” · etirflita — “Dodged that bullet”

etirflita (on the summer muster) — “Did you start mocking up everyone’s tombstones for this campaign? I feel like we’re going to be spamming #cemetery” · ArcticFox — “PLANKS FOR EVERYONE!” · etirflita — “Planks for the memories!”

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “this is a super low fatality group. you guys don’t seem to want to murder each other much” · etirflita — “I went from 3 to 6, then 8, now 3 again. … It’s quicker to die constantly and luck out with a good chargen roll”

Curtis Sinnoch — “I’ve been trying my darndest to level people up to my SL … and now with @WillWorker up here, its not so lonely!” · WillWorker — “The knighthood was an unexpected boost, but it has been quite the slog going from SL3 to SL10.”

❦

Summer 1625 — Slaughter in the Woods of Nantes

June – August 1625 · resolved at the table 8–10 November 2020 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: the forests of Western France, outside Nantes

The situation

Nantes had fallen in the spring; the Huguenots now held the whole Atlantic seaboard. On the Sunday evening the Minister of War drew up the summer’s deployment, and the table learned that nobody was staying home:

Narrator — “The Minister of War decides who to draw up for the Summer Campaign.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Everyone except the 4th Foot Brigade is going on campaign.” — “That means everyone, since Pain Loafmann is the only one without a regiment and he is being volunteered.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “Field Operations for everybody!”

Jean Rochant — “YAY! WE ALL GET TO DIE!”

The Narrator set the strategic scene the next night:

Narrator — “The Huguenot forces have spread throughout Western France, controlling the coastline north of Aquitaine. Even Bordeaux has fallen after the disastrous Spring Campaign.”

Narrator — “With the French Navy mostly wiped out in the Winter Campaign, Cardinal Richelieu has made serious concessions to England and the Dutch in return for assistance against their Protestant brothers, in the form of loaned ships to be manned by French crews.”

Narrator — “As Vice-Admiral Van Dorp roams up and down the Atlantic coast, seeking to engage the Protestant Navy, the Field Army of France has been deployed to root out and eliminate the scattered Protestant militias which have taken hold of the coast.”

The order of battle

The army’s commander was an old acquaintance — the man who had already fumbled one summer:

Narrator — “Despite his poor showing last summer, the Field Marshal has placed the Field Army under the command of General Gilles Barbier once more.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “@ArcticFox this is for Fame, Fortune, and the glory of Paris!”

Henri Toulouse — “It’s for your maman.”

Narrator — “Unable to maintain significant tactical control during such a far-ranging campaign, General Barbier is relegated to making high-level operational and strategic decisions. His adjutant, the colonel of the 69th Arquebusiers, is well-known for being a very large fish from a very small pond.”

Under Barbier stood the whole Field Army: the 1st Division (Lt. General Pierre-Paul Auclair) in the center, the 2nd Division (Lt. General Hervé Dufau, the General’s nephew), the Frontier Division scattered forward as scouts, and the Cavalry Division (Lt. General Jérémie De Michele) on the left — with the Guards Brigade, Dragoon Brigade, Horse Guards, Heavy Brigade and three Brigades of Foot filling the line. Every player character marched:

Character Post Unit
Sir Hercule Bonaparte Commanding 3rd Squadron Crown Prince Cuirassiers
Curtis Sinnoch Major, commanding his battalion King’s Musketeers
Jules Lavelle Brigade Major to Bg. Gen. Honoré Boulle Horse Guards
Pierre de Tête Brigade Major to Bg. Gen. Sylvestre Laurens Dragoon Brigade
Alphonse Devereux Officer Royal Marines
Jean Rochant Officer Royal Marines
Lucien Vaillancourt Subaltern Picardy Musketeers, 1st Brigade of Foot
Henri Toulouse Subaltern 53rd Fusiliers
Clemens Charron Subaltern 13th Fusiliers
Pain Loafmann Convict volunteer, sentence being served Frontier Regiment, Frontier Division

The record

Narrator — “The Summer Campaign begins.”

Narrator — “The General gathers his division commanders around him and gives them their orders.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “While uninspired, the General’s command that divisions are to move as one, so that they can reinforce each other should one falter, is what the textbook says, at least where it comes to open-field warfare. The area being heavily forested is no deterrent.” — “The result is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “1st Division Commander Lt. General Pierre-Paul Auclair has been assigned the honor of leading the center. A dependable but unimaginable soldier, he passes the General Barbier’s orders on to his brigadiers and say ‘You know what to do.’”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “Advancing forward steadily, the occasional encampment is found and destroyed, but many more are found abandoned, the militias long having made their escape.” — “The result is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “2nd Division Commander Lt. General Hervé Dufau is General Barbier’s nephew, and no better suited to his position than his uncle. He orders his men to set fire to the woods behind the division, the better to spur them forward.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “His adjutant reassures the brigadiers that Dufau was merely kidding. The men continue the steady advance.” — “The result is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “The Frontier Division, with its new recruit [Pain Loafmann], is split up and scattered in front of the other divisions, to act as forward scouts.” — “Its commander this season, Jean-François Vayssière, is a sentenced prisoner of some high status, but a military man by no means.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “When asked by the Frontier Regiment’s Colonel how to best advance, he is heard to retort, ‘Certainly not by joining the Frontier Regiment, sir.’” — “The Frontier Division is picked apart and driven from the field by camouflaged rebels.”

Narrator — “The Cavalry Division has been assigned the left flank. Lt. General Jérémie De Michele instructs his men to dismount and lead their horses until the battle is joined, to keep pace with the other flanks as ordered.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “The enemy are quick to note the approaching horses, but not as quick as if the horses were being ridden hard. It is all too easy to run down the fleeing rebels as they hurriedly pack their camps. The enemy on the left flank is driven from the field.”

Narrator — “The Dragoon Brigade, reporting directly to the General, is tasked with responding quickly to fill any holes, and so deployed to cover for the retreating Frontier Division.” — “Bg. General Sylvestre Laurens isn’t excited about these orders, but not much excites the old salt anymore.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “Unlike most of the cavalry brigades, the dragoons are trained to dismount before engaging. They find themselves shot to pieces as they struggle to leap off their horses safely in the heavy forest. They, too, are driven from the field.”

Then came the passage that turned a bad day into an atrocity — the Guards firing on their own:

Narrator — “The Guards Brigade have seen little trouble. Bg. General Yvan Bellefeuille is dissatisfied with this, and hungers for glory. He orders the Guards to fire indiscriminately into the woods to frighten the rebels into surrender.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The Guards pick their targets with great relish. The targets turn out to be retreating members of the Frontier Division, who scream for help as they die. This in turn draws out the Dragoon Brigade, who enter into heavy combat with the Guards. The Dragoons enjoy a pyrrhic victory. The Guards are crushed.”

Narrator — “The 1st Brigade of Foot, led by the redoubtable Bg. General Robert Vasseur, sees the ruin facing the center flank. Vasseur realizes the success of the day rides on his shoulders.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “Some are lost, but many are saved. Vasseur leads his men to a fighting stalemate, and at the very least, holds the ground.” — “The result is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “On the right flank, the 2nd Brigade commander, Bg. Gen. Xavier Cherbuliez, receives word of a fortified position just ahead, atop a large hill. There is mention of cannons.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The mention turns out to be correct. Cherbuliez tries to array his regiments for an assault on the hill, but organization in the forest is too much of a challenge even for his adroit military mind. Some cannons pick off a few men, but the rest take cover, unable to advance. The result is Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “The 3rd Brigade of Foot, under the command of the substantially-less-able Bg. Gen Auguste Delacroix, sees the same hill from another angle.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Delacroix trusts in his men to organize themselves. But little is more organized than a cannonball careening through ranks of hapless footmen. The 3rd Brigade suffers heavy casualties, and is driven from the field.”

With the vast Protestant army at its gates, La Rochelle’s city council votes to join Duke Soubise. Aftermath

Word of Pierre’s death reached the streets of Paris before the army did:

A plank went up in the cemetery, and Curtis Sinnoch left flowers on it:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah Pierre, gone too soon! You were a rising star, and I had hoped to help elevate you to greatness!”

The same night his character died, DancingHorse was already rolling dice in the chargen channel: “Coming soon to Paris, the second son of a Knight, the very wealthy Amand Baschet.” The name would land like a rapier-thrust of its own — for the army stayed in the field into the autumn, and in September a letter caught up with Jean Rochant:

Jean Rochant — “receives a letter from Mlle Blaise informing him that she could no longer stand waiting, after four long months of being unable to see him due to military service. She mentioned in passing being enamored with a new suitor, a Mr. Armand Baschet. It was a name that seemed to ring a distant bell, but now was one committed to memory.”

Lucien Vaillancourt — “@etirflita receives a letter from the bawdyhouse informing him that after four long months, his spot on the wall of ‘customer of the year’ has been replaced by another John. Also they seem to have fewer crabs (perhaps due to his absence.)”
