❦

Fall 1625 — Île de Ré: Not a Single (Enemy-Inflicted) Casualty

September – November 1625 · resolved at the table 2–3 December 2020 · Mission: Siege · Theater: Île de Ré, off the Atlantic coast of France

The situation

Summer had ended in catastrophe: the woods outside Nantes had swallowed thousands of the King’s men, La Rochelle had gone over to the rebels, and the Duke of Soubise had crowned himself Lord Protector of a brand-new French Protestant Republic. The fall campaign would be France’s answer. On the evening of 2 December the Narrator set the board:

Narrator — “The Protestant Navy, having taken the coast, quickly moved to establish control over strategic islands it could use as ports of refuge from royal reinforcements. One island in particular, Île de Ré, has become a central gathering point from which the Huguenots launch raids on unsuspecting French towns.”

Narrator — “The Dutch and English have once again lent their warships to the cause. the Duke of Montmorency requests the Frontier Regiments to aid in capturing Ré.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1 (the table winced)

Narrator — “A fierce naval battle ensues. Although several Dutch ships are lost with all hands, the Protestant Navy is finally crushed. Soubise retreats to the fortress on the island’s northwest point. English ships deliver the Frontier Regiments to the island’s unprotected southern beaches and wish them Godspeed.”

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiments will undertake a Siege.”

The Royal Foot Guards were not even supposed to be there — they had volunteered themselves. Two weeks earlier, in the off-season channel, their acting commander had read the newspapers and made a decision:

Sir Hercule Bonaparte — “Excellent, Capt. @Jules Lavelle. Having read correspondence from the front, I am underwhelmed by our progress. After these last few campaigns, France is in need of a grand victory to ensure neighboring nations do not begin to question our military might. Now is not the time for its greatest fighting force to be sitting on the side lines. Please have the appropriate dispatches prepared and sent, advising the men to indulge themselves this October, for November we march to the aid of our countrymen.”

Jules Lavelle — “Yes, sir!!”

Sir Hercule Bonaparte — “@Curtis Sinnoch @Jules Lavelle and the rest of the Royal Foot Guard: I trust you all took advantage of your respite in October. Now let us go and remind France and the world that it has one of the greatest fighting forces on the planet.”

The order of battle

The Narrator introduced the siege’s senior officer with a single, complete biography:

Narrator — “Col. Jean-Pascal Boucher, currently assigned to lead the Frontier Regiment, is a man of uncommon tactical brilliance who happened to tell an off-color joke about the Queen at the wrong time.”

Narrator — “Cols. Boucher and Devereux, alongside Lt. Col. Bonaparte, agree to arrange their forces into a trident, with Boucher leading the central thrust and the Royal Regiments on the flanks.”

Character Post Where in the trident
Col. Alphonse Devereux Commanding the Royal Marines right flank
Lt. Col. Sir Hercule Bonaparte Commanding the Royal Foot Guards left flank
Maj. Curtis Sinnoch Brigade Major, Guards Brigade (King’s Musketeers) with the Royal Regiments on the flank
Capt. Jules Lavelle Regimental adjunct to Bonaparte, Royal Foot Guards left flank
Jean Rochant Royal Marines right flank
Subaltern Lucien Vaillancourt Picardy Musketeers central thrust, under Boucher
Subaltern Clemens Charron 13th Fusiliers central thrust, under Boucher
Pain Loafmann Convict volunteer, Frontier Regiment central thrust, under Boucher

Pain Loafmann was at the front by court order. A month earlier, in the streets of Paris, the Narrator had set his terms: “If tried and convicted, Pain Loafmann faces a maximum sentence of 1 year’s service in the Frontier Regiments.” Remember that sentence.

The record

The central thrust went first.

Narrator — “The island offers poor cover for advancing on a fortified position. Boucher orders his battalions into loose formations, to diminish the effectiveness of enemy batteries.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “The tactic works even better than expected. The Frontier Regiment attains the walls of the fort without suffering a single casualty, as the panicking defenders have failed to properly calibrate their cannons.”

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiments have succeeded in their portion of the Siege, with a result of 1.”

A perfect assault — a decisive victory without one man lost to the enemy. The men of the central thrust settled their personal fates on the spot (see The reckoning below, where that phrase “not a single casualty” gets its asterisk). Then the left flank stirred:

Narrator — “@WillWorker, please roll for your battle result.” — “Your forces are prepared to advance on the flank.”

Sir Hercule Bonaparte — “Alright men, the enemy is cornered, so now is the time for patience. There is no need for unnecessary recklessness, we control the tempo, not them. And while I know many of you will wish this as an opportunity for vengeance, when you encounter cowards you are to show them mercy and take them prisoner. Do not give our quarry reason to believe they must fight in desperation to the last. Show them a golden bridge on which they may have some hope of seeing tomorrow and their morale shall crumble with barely any effort on our part.”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “Your men follow your words, occasionally glancing over at the center and right flank which have already begun to advance. Yet your speech is simply too engrossing. By the time you finish and your men catch up, the siege is mostly over. Your result is Inconclusive, a 4.”

On the right flank, the Royal Marines waited until evening for their moment. Jean Rochant grew impatient:

Jean Rochant — “@Anonymouse, We wish to bring our own glory to France! When do we enter the fray!?”

Col. Alphonse Devereux — “Onward, men! For France!”

🎲 Alphonse Devereux rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The Royal Marines were expecting an amphibious assault, not one made parallel to the shore. They charge forward, but slip and fall over the uneven sandy expanse that covers their flank, making little progress. This is an Inconclusive result of 4.”

And that was the whole siege: the center took the walls flawlessly while one flank listened to a speech and the other fell over in the sand. Soubise’s fortress fell, and the Duke himself was taken — the capture confirmed in the Narrator’s own words a day later (“the Duke had the wine poisoned before his capture”) and again at the opening of the winter campaign: “in the wake of the capture of the Duke of Soubise, the last remaining Protestant strongholds have been captured and demilitarized.”

A year’s service in the Frontier Regiments, the court had said. Pain Loafmann — Dutch bread-futures heir, accused murderer, the campaign’s perennial volunteer — served roughly six months of it before falling at the front of the central thrust, in the assault that officially cost France nothing. Col. Alphonse Devereux — “Well, I guess I can’t complain too much.”

The poisoned cellar

The next evening the Narrator delivered the campaign’s NPC casualty report — all of it dated to the victory itself:

Narrator — “In the middle of the victory party, the 2nd Battalion Commander of the Cardinal’s Guard and his captains sneak away from the official celebration to break into the Duke of Soubise’s wine supply. Unfortunately, it is discovered that the Duke had the wine poisoned before his capture, when all three men are found dead the next morning.” (reactions: grim ×4)

Jules Lavelle — “Ah my bad”

The ledger

  • Dead: Subaltern Lucien Vaillancourt (rotten plank); Pain Loafmann (at the front, sentence discharged); the Cardinal’s Guard 2nd Battalion commander and his captains (Soubise’s wine).
  • Captured: the Duke of Soubise, Lord Protector of the French Protestant Republic.
  • Promoted: Clemens Charron to Captain, 13th Fusiliers; Hercule Bonaparte to brevet Colonel, Royal Foot Guards; Jean Rochant to Subaltern, Royal Marines.
  • Mentioned in despatches: Curtis Sinnoch (+4 status; 300 crowns; failed his title roll — needed 3, rolled 1).
  • Plunder: the Duke of Soubise’s personal tea service, 1400 crowns (Clemens Charron); 600 crowns (Bonaparte); a Barbary Coast pirate’s buried treasure, 200 crowns (Jean Rochant).

In the announcements channel that night, the Narrator closed the month with what may be the driest line of the whole campaign:

Narrator — “November passes without incident.”

Aftermath

On 3 December the Narrator rolled the winter volunteer lottery, regiment by regiment — CG 3, KM 4, DG 3, QOC 4, ALC 5, CPC 1, PM 4:

Narrator — “no regiments are going. I’ll roll the battalions separately and let you know” — “Battalions volunteering: Cardinal’s Guard, 1st / Queen’s Own Carabiniers, 3rd / Archduke Leopold’s Cuirassiers, 2nd (no players affected)”

Jean Rochant — “Phew”

The Narrator had already teased the table about it the night before: “unless you want to volunteer for winter.... but you might make poor Jean sad.” Alphonse’s answer: “Nope. Not going to volunteer for Winter.” Even so, on 8 December the campaign channel carried one more announcement — “@harry has volunteered for the Frontier Regiment” — Captain Charron, tea service in hand, heading back toward the guns of his own free will.

The same afternoon Lucien died, his player’s next man was already strolling the streets of Paris:

Marius Thibodeaux — “I have just arrived in Paris and already feel a bout of ennui overtaking me. What do you people do for fun here?” (reactions: plank ×3)

And on the 7th, the heroes came home from Ré to discover what Paris had been doing while they bled — Amand Baschet had spent the fall courting Jean’s mistress:

Jean Rochant — “I return from the front, after honorably defending France under the command of @Anonymouse only to discovery that my dearest Adelaide Blaise has been stolen away by the dishonorable snake @DancingHorse, who took advantage of my service to king and country to take advantage and become, as the colloquialism in the colonies goes ‘Mr. Steal Yo’ Girl.’ As a result, I issue a December challenge to @DancingHorse, while not for Mme. Blaise’s hand, but for his dishonorable actions.”

Jean Rochant — “And as you will note, I do not blame Adelaide, but rather that coward @DancingHorse, who sits in Paris while the rest of us stumble on the beaches and fight for country!”

Jules Lavelle — “He is a cavalryman; he was in need of a sturdy mount.”

That duel is winter’s story.

Winter 1625–26 — No Quarter on the Sèvre Niortaise

December 1625 – February 1626 · resolved at the table 30 December 2020 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: the West Coast of France — the Sèvre Niortaise, Poitou

The situation

The fall had broken the rebellion’s back: the Duke of Soubise taken at Île de Ré, his fleet scattered, his strongholds falling one by one. What remained was not a war but a hunt. On the night of 30 December the Narrator opened the ledger on The Winter Campaign:

Narrator — “Aided by the Dutch and English ships, in the wake of the capture of the Duke of Soubise, the last remaining Protestant strongholds have been captured and demilitarized. All that remains is to scour the countryside for scattered pockets of traitor militia.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for the mission — 6

Narrator — “As such, all regiments will be performing Field Operations, hunting down the traitors wherever they may be found along the West Coast.”

The decision to march had been taken weeks earlier, in the Royal Foot Guards’ mess, where the regiment’s brevet Colonel put the question to his officers:

Hercule Bonaparte — “While I am loathe to leave the comforts of Paris to campaign in the winter, three battalions is a paltry amount to be fielding. What say you gentlemen? With so few on the field, there would be plenty of glory for us. Shall we enjoy our Christmas now and greet the New Year showing France the true grit of its Royal Guard.”

Jules Lavelle — “Sir, wherever you go, I shall follow. To glory!!”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Personally, I am in favor of staying in Paris for both Dec and Jan - One of the best ways to stay warm is the company of our fine wines and finer ladies! Let the rebels squander their meager rations and hope, and when they are most stretched thin, we ride in Feb and crush them like the insects they are!”

Christmas was duly enjoyed, and by mid-January the regiment was pawing at the ground:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah, my fellows - it was a lovely Christmas and New Year spent at home, but I feel like, well rested, it is time for us to march to the front! What say you, commander? What shall your orders be?”

Jules Lavelle — “I stand ready as always to lay my life on the line for France!”

Hercule Bonaparte — “I believe I made my intentions clear. We shall not delay France’s glory a moment longer.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Thank you for the confirmation - this February, we shall ride and bring a swift victory for France!”

The order of battle

Narrator — “WillWorker [Hercule Bonaparte], Curtis Sinnoch, and Jules Lavelle are deployed with the rest of the Royal Foot Guards. Anonymouse [Alphonse Devereux] and harry [Clemens Charron] are attached to the Frontier Regiment under the command of Col. Jean-Yves De la Croix, currently serving out a sentence for mass public indecency for a drunken Noel dare gone wrong.”

(The table saluted Colonel De la Croix’s vignette with 🎅 🍆 🥶.)

Clemens Charron had put his name forward at the start of December — “harry has volunteered for the Frontier Regiment,” the Narrator recorded. Alphonse Devereux, Colonel of the Royal Marines, marched without his regiment. When Marius Thibodeaux — the new major Alphonse had personally welcomed into the Marines a month before (“That’s a sweet MA! Join us, and fight for France!”) — asked whether the Marines were headed to war as well, the answer from on high was terse:

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “Alphonse has gone to war by himself”

Marius Thibodeaux — “C’est la vie.”

Character Rank / post Unit
Hercule Bonaparte Brevet Colonel, commanding the regiment Royal Foot Guards
Curtis Sinnoch Major, on the Brigade-General’s staff with the Royal Foot Guards
Jules Lavelle Regimental adjunct Royal Foot Guards
Alphonse Devereux Colonel of the Royal Marines, gone to war alone attached to the Frontier Regiment
Clemens Charron Volunteer Frontier Regiment

Opposite them: scattered pockets of traitor militia, the last armed Protestants of the West Coast — and, for the Frontier Regiment, Col. Jean-Yves De la Croix, commanding his unwilling volunteers between installments of his sentence.

The record

The Royal Foot Guards at the river

Narrator — “Independently ranging, the Royal Foot Guards happen upon a contingent of Protestant troops waiting to ferry across the Sèvre Niortaise. Once across, the traitors are sure to burn the boats, eliminating any chance of capture. WillWorker, what happens next is up to you.”

Hercule Bonaparte — “Jules Lavelle — Prepare and send dispatches for a full assault on their flank opposite the Frontier Regiment. Tell the men to offer no quarter and an make examples of those who do not flee in terror. We shall drive them from the shoreline away from their boats in the waiting arms of capture by the Frontier Regiment. And, if any do elude us, the memory of what happens to traitors will be forever etched into their minds, such that they will never raise a hand against France again. Task and position Curtis Sinnoch with the important duty of securing any path back to their boats once we have pushed them away from the shore. Last thing we want is some thinking they can double back.”

Jules Lavelle — “Yes, sir! On the nonce!”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 5

Narrator — “The enemy stands utterly unprepared to do battle. The first volley launched from ambush rips through the men already on boats, leaving their broken bodies strewn across the damaged rivercraft. The rest of the enemy force is thrown into disarray, unable to organize itself to clear the boats and make haste, nor to mount any kind of meaningful counterstrike. A few poltroons swim for it, avoiding the enemy’s complete annihilation, but nothing of any significance remains intact on the field. Your battle result is a 2.”

Hercule Bonaparte — “Excellent work, men!”

The Frontier Regiment in the countryside

The Guards’ personal reckonings were tallied first (see below); then the Narrator turned to the other column:

Narrator — “Attempting to maneuver his volunteers in concert with the expertly-drilled Royal Foot Guards, Col. Jean-Yves De la Croix orders a series of overlapping formations to spread out across the countryside.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for De la Croix’s command — 5

Narrator — “Only small pockets of resistance are encountered, and most of the unwilling volunteers decline to seriously engage them. Where they do fight, it goes poorly for the Frontier troops. The battle result is a 4, Inconclusive.”

Alphonse Devereux — Colonel of the Royal Marines, with the Frontier Regiment

The Frontier column’s inconclusive skirmishing offered thin pickings — the Narrator posted Clemens Charron’s grim numbers (“harry, your rolls are Death 8, Mention 12, Promotion 8, Crowns 9,” met with a 😟) — and then turned to the colonel who had gone to war by himself. Alphonse’s player had left the table, and had left instructions. The Narrator rolled the dice himself:

Narrator — “Alphonse throws himself at the enemy with no regard for his own survival. His death roll is a 9, and he opts for reckless bravery of 5.”

So fell one of the original gentlemen of March 1624 — duelist, Colonel of Marines, host of the Blue Gables, the man whose father once carried a leg of seasoned lamb across Paris to see how his little Alphonse was doing in the big city.

Clemens Charron — Volunteer, Frontier Regiment

Clemens Charron — “After these many long months of service, I have had many chances to observe man’s frailty. This may be the day I experience my own. Let fate decide.”

Narrator — “An uneventful season!”

Honors and the ledger

The no-quarter ambush on the Sèvre Niortaise made the Royal Foot Guards’ winter. Hercule Bonaparte came home mentioned in despatches a fourth time (+6 SP for three months), promoted to full Colonel of the Royal Foot Guards, and 400 crowns richer from the Protestant payroll — though the King declined to add a title to the pile. Curtis Sinnoch was mentioned a fifth time, promoted to Lieutenant Colonel, released from the Brigade-General’s staff, and intercepted the bulk of the traitors’ payroll on the road: 700 crowns. Jules Lavelle took a brevet Majority and 500 crowns, and traded the adjunct’s desk for battalion command. In the Frontier Regiment, Clemens Charron marched home unpaid, unmentioned, and alive — by his own accounting, good news. Alphonse Devereux did not march home at all.

Spring 1626 — One Small Iron Lock: The Siege of Poschiavo

April–May 1626 · resolved at the table 25–26 January 2021 · Mission: Siege · Theater: the Valtellina — the fortified town of Poschiavo, in the Alps

The situation

A year and a half after the gentlemen first bled in the Alpine valley (see Fall 1624), the war for the Valtellina resumed. On the night of 25 January the Narrator opened The Spring Campaign with the fullest statement of the war’s stakes the game ever received:

Narrator — “For many years France has worked to keep separate the many branches of the sprawling Habsburg dynasty. The insidious family’s control of Spain and the Holy Roman Empire holds France in a vise, and only Cardinal Richelieu’s deft maneuvering has kept its jaws from shutting closed. The Habsburgs seek to unite their empire across the Alps, and the corridor for this joining runs through the valley of Valtelline.”

Narrator — “Although the people of Valtellina themselves are Protestant, the Swiss Grison League has aligned itself with France to liberate the region from the hands of the Spanish and Papal armies occupying the valley. With Venice and Savoy also providing support, this is France’s best chance to drive a wedge between the Habsburgs and maintain its strategic position in Europe – a cause worth cooperating with a few Protestants over.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for the mission — 1

Narrator — “This is a Siege campaign. The town of Poschiavo has been fortified and must be taken. Whoever controls Poschiavo controls Valtellina.”

The expedition itself had been raised weeks earlier, in the April rain, by volunteers. Major Marius Thibodeaux of the Royal Marines had been planning it since the winter thaw:

Marius Thibodeaux — “@ArcticFox what say you to fighting for our country in May? There’s too much rain in April to do much but loaf about and drink. By May we should be invigorated and our hearts will be overflowing with wine and patriotism!”

Jules Lavelle — “Look at this guy seeking advice from a…subaltern??? Such bonhomie in the Royal Marines; can’t be good for morale.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “This man is my senior in that he has battle experience with this regiment, I am still working my way through the ranks (admittedly at a frenzied pace). I am only just getting the measure of Paris, and I respect the interests of my men.” — “Also, having just started a courtship myself with the lovely Angélique Beauchamp (and hearing sordid tales of the last time Jean was deployed), I wanted to allow him to have a month’s warning in order to fully satisfy any curiosities his mistress might have in his absence”

A week later the papers were signed:

Marius Thibodeaux — “The Royal Marines intend to serve our great nation in May!” — “Then yep, I volunteer the 1st Battalion”

Jules Lavelle — “@WillWorker, the Royal Foot Guards’ 2nd Battalion will be deploying, whether or not the regiment goes, though I do hope to serve once more under your inspiring leadership.”

Hercule Bonaparte — “@Jules Lavelle Of course the regiment shall be marching in full. I wouldn’t have it any other way. We can have our mettle getting rusty, can we?”

Jean Rochant — “Wait, I’m A Company, but I dont have a batallion listed for myself?” — “Am I going on campaign?”

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “A Company is always in 1st Battalion” — “so yes, you are going” (saluted with the campaign’s traditional plank, ×4)

Curtis Sinnoch — “TO WAR!”

The order of battle

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards will be taking the field, serving alongside the Frontier Regiments, led by the arrogant blowhard Col. Gilles Morançais, himself the abandoned bastard son of a disgraced duke, in flight from gambling arrears.”

Character Rank / post Unit
Hercule Bonaparte Colonel, commanding the regiment Royal Foot Guards
Curtis Sinnoch Lieutenant Colonel, commanding 1st Battalion Royal Foot Guards
Jules Lavelle Brevet Major, commanding 2nd Battalion Royal Foot Guards
Marius Thibodeaux Major, commanding 1st Battalion (volunteered) Royal Marines
Jean Rochant Subaltern, A Company, 1st Battalion Royal Marines

Opposite them: the Spanish and Papal garrison of Poschiavo, behind fresh fortifications — with the armies of Venice and Savoy operating on the far side of the valley, and Col. Gilles Morançais and his Frontier Regiments as France’s dubious contribution to allied coordination.

The record

The planning tent

Narrator — “@WillWorker, Gilles Morançais meets you in the planning tent and offers his opinion of the tactical situation. ‘Right up the middle,’ he declares. ‘Spaniards and Italians. Good for basking in the sun, and not much else. We’ll rout them with ease.’” (the table: ☀️ ☀️)

Hercule Bonaparte — “Then lets use that laziness to our advantage. This is a siege after all. It’s their job to stew nervously waiting for us to act. The forces of Venice and Savoy are taking care of their side of the valley, we’ll take care of ours. Send messengers advising everyone in the Valley that once the siege begins in earnest, any hint of resistance will be offered no quarter. But for now, anyone who wants to leave the Valley unarmed and in peace may do so.

@Curtis Sinnoch Among your other duties, I’m giving you the responsibility over scouting. Ensure we know well in advance of any approaching Spanish reinforcements. Last thing I want to do is get pincered in the very valley we’re invading.

Fret not, Col. Morançais. We’ll be going right up the middle. There’s nowhere else to go. Just after we’ve given them a few days to voluntarily thin their ranks.”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 3

Whereupon the entire alliance discovered, together, what nobody had packed:

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards take up their positions facing the city walls, taking pot shots here and there, but there seems to be no clear path into the town. Siege equipment and cannon would have been helpful, but each member of the alliance seems to be under the impression that the others were supposed to bring those. The soldiery wastes rounds fighting, and there are a few casualties due to return fire from the walls, but nothing serious. The battle result is a 3 - Inconclusive.”

The 2nd Battalion at the wall

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch will roll for command of 1st Battalion. @Jules Lavelle will roll for command of 2nd Battalion.”

Jules Lavelle — “The 2nd Battalion has been training for weeks climbing bridges around Paris. No mere town wall can keep us out! Men, charge!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 5

Narrator — “The 2nd Battalion rushes forward, inexpertly guided by their novice commander. They reach the wall, and some find a handhold, but they are ultimately turned back by a hail of rocks from above. The battle result is a 3 - Inconclusive.”

At the stroke of midnight the Narrator posted the colonel’s and the major’s personal fortunes — Jules Lavelle rolled his on the spot (see The reckoning) — and then the siege resumed, with the allies’ finest tactician leading from the front:

Morançais goes up the middle

Narrator — “While we await the other Royal Foot Guards, Col. Col. Morançais makes his move, sending his men charging headlong into the gate.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for Morançais’s command — 5

Narrator — “Unsurprisingly, they fare little better than the Foot Guards’ 2nd Battalion, though they take a few more hits in the open approach. The result is a 4 - Inconclusive.”

Marius at the gate

Narrator — “@etirflita, roll to see your battalion through the charge!”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 5

Narrator — “The bodies of hapless Frontier Regiment soldiers fall to your right and left, but your men stay true. You rush to the gate, suffering few losses, and find it a hasty construction, nothing but the thinnest of wooden planks, with one small iron lock. You smash the lock with the hilt of your cutlass and send the confused Papal soldiers within running for their lives as the gate crashes open. Your result is a 2 - Success with a storming party.”

Narrator — “Marius, gain 1 MA for outperforming your commander.” (💪)

Curtis’s volley

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch , go ahead”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 6

Narrator — “Where the 2nd Battalion trained to climb, the 1st wisely trained to fight. Your men duck and weave on their approach, expertly dodging enemy fire. As the Royal Marines battalion approaches the gates, you spy a squad of Spaniards lugging a vat of flaming grease to the top of the wall. With one word, your men cease their mad dash and fall to their knees, taking careful aim and firing a volley as one, felling the Spanish devils and setting the top of the wall aflame with spilled grease. Thanks to you, the siege has been a success! Your battle result is 1.”

Narrator — “Gain 1 MA for outperforming your commander.” (💪)

Poschiavo had fallen — the gate smashed open by a Marine major’s cutlass hilt, the wall-top cleared by a single kneeling volley, and not a cannon fired because not a cannon had come. Whoever controls Poschiavo controls Valtellina.

The reckoning

Jules Lavelle — Brevet Major, 2nd Battalion, Royal Foot Guards

Narrator — “@Jules Lavelle , your rolls are Death 16, Mention 12, Promotion 11, Crowns 7”

Jules Lavelle — “Of course, a Lavelle knows something of discretion in valor. Reckless bravery of 4!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 2d6 (#Death, target 16) — 7 (survives)

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 2d6 (#Mention, target 12, +4 bravery) — 11

Narrator — “Mentioned!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 2d6 (#Promotion, target 11) — 6 (no promotion)

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 2d6 (#Crowns, target 7, +4 bravery) — 4

Narrator — “Looted!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 1d6 for mention status — 3

Narrator — “+3 sp for 3 months”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 2d6 for loot — 11

Narrator — “You have managed to seize some gems from the town’s coat of arms worth 1100 crowns.”

Marius Thibodeaux — Major, 1st Battalion, Royal Marines

Narrator — “@etirflita , your rolls are Death 12, Mention 8, Promotion 6, Crowns 6”

The man who smashed the gate declined reckless bravery and rolled straight:

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 2d6 (Death, target 12) — 7

Narrator — “Alive!”

Honors and the ledger

Not one Frenchman of note died before Poschiavo, and the loot rained down in a way no campaign of the game had yet matched: Jean Rochant, an unmentioned subaltern, walked off with 1,800 crowns — the biggest single haul — followed by Jules Lavelle (mentioned in despatches, +3 SP for three months, and 1,100 crowns in gems prised from the town’s coat of arms), Hercule Bonaparte (900 crowns and a resolution to do better), Curtis Sinnoch (his sixth mention, +5 SP, 800 crowns, and a Knighthood from the King — worth 10 extra status points that month for his great prestige), and Marius Thibodeaux, first man into the town, who took 700 crowns of the Papal treasure and +1 MA for outperforming his commander, a distinction he shared with Sir Curtis. (The Narrator’s last act of the night was a quiet unexplained bookkeeping roll — 2d6 “#RMcapA,” a 2 — and nothing more was said of it.)

The money did not stay idle. The next morning — Narrator: “The Minister of War, satisfied with the siege of Valtellina, prepares the Summer Campaign.” — the victors of Poschiavo went shopping. Jean Rochant (“I may as well spend some of these riches before I die”) bought a captaincy of E Company and then a majority on top of it, mounting himself on a horse named “Dice Daemon.” Marius Thibodeaux, freshly SL 6, bought the open Lieutenant-Colonelcy for 140 crowns — “A bargain!” — making him, per the GM, acting regimental commander of the Royal Marines.

Summer 1626 — Rennes: “His Moustaches Are Rather Inspiring”

June – August 1626 · resolved at the table 22–23 February 2021 · Mission: Siege · Theater: Rennes, Brittany

The situation

This was the summer the war turned inward. No Habsburgs, no Huguenot republic — the enemy this season was a fortified French city sheltering French conspirators from a French cardinal. The Narrator set the board just before midnight on 22 February:

Narrator — “The Comte de Chalais, Henri de Talleyrand-Périgord, stands accused by Cardinal Richelieu of conspiring to assassinate His Eminence. As the investigation began, the Comte fled to the fortress of the Governor of Brittany and Duke of Vendôme, César de Bourbon, the king’s own half-brother.”

Narrator — “Cardinal Richelieu has asked the Minister of War to send a small task force to retrieve these and several other conspirators from Rennes, where they’ve gone into hiding. Brittany’s governor is not without some military might of his own, and as the contingent of Royal troops approaches the city, it becomes clear that they are approaching a fortified target.”

The call-up had come weeks earlier in the off-season channel, on the heels of the spring victory in the Valtellina:

Narrator — “The Minister of War, satisfied with the siege of Valtellina, prepares the Summer Campaign.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The following brigades will be called up: 1st Foot Brigade, 3rd Foot Brigade, 4th Foot Brigade, Frontier Regiments, RNHB Regiment”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “this will be a Siege campaign.” (Players affected by call: Marius Thibodeaux, Jean Rochant, Guerinet Durand.)

The Marines went shopping before they went to war. Marius bought the open Lieutenant-Colonelcy of the Royal Marines for 140 crowns — “A bargain!” — which made him acting regimental commander; Jean Rochant bought the majority that fell vacant beneath him, “120 crowns + 200 more for horses.” His new horse was named “Dice Daemon.” From the interim months at the front, one dispatch survives:

Jean Rochant — “Meanwhile Mjr. Rochant is leading his troops, helping them avoid planks and cannonballs in the field of battle”

Not everyone got to go. Col. Hercule Bonaparte volunteered the Royal Foot Guards for the summer and was overruled by the Minister of War himself, in a letter that caught his carriage just before it left Paris:

Narrator — “Col. Bonaparte, It buoys my heart to know of the resolve and zeal with which you would commit your men to the defense of France. However, France is large, and I have already apportioned the appropriate units for the summer campaign. I have determined that your unit is needed to maintain the defense of Paris; as you know, the security of the capital is our chief concern in the face of heavy Habsburg maneuvering. When the danger is less, you are welcome to volunteer your men freely in another season. But for this summer, at least, you are staying put. With resolve, Minister Rouvier”

Hercule Bonaparte — “Hercule turns his carriage around and returns home to spend the month planning a new campaign with his beau: if the Minister of War refuses his brilliance, then the only solution is to become the Minister of War.” (four 😮 reactions)

In the announcements channel, the month of the assault reads, in full: “August 1626 — The month passes uneventfully in Paris.”

The order of battle

The Narrator introduced the chain of command from the top down, one vignette apiece:

Narrator — “The Field Army is commanded by General Michael Masse, a competent enough leader, known for his reliability, decorum, and above all, luxurious moustaches.”

Narrator — “The 1st Division is under the leadership of Lt. General Pierre-Paul Auclair, another well-regarded sort, known for a series of clever victories a decade back that he still coasts on to this day.”

Narrator — “The 1st Brigade is commanded by Bg. General Robert Vasseur, who is understood to be the finest foot commander the French Army has to offer.”

Narrator — “General Masse chooses, for his adjutant, the Colonel of the 69th Arquebusiers.”

Narrator — “Lt. General Auclair selects the Lt. Colonel of the Gascon Regiment for his adjutant.”

Narrator — “Maj. Augustin Ladagnous is already serving as the Brigade Major to Bg. General Vasseur.”

Narrator — “Marius Thibodeaux commands the Royal Marines. Sylvestre Côté commands the Picardy Musketeers.”

Character Post Where
Lt. Col. Marius Thibodeaux Acting commander, Royal Marines 1st Brigade, on the main road
Maj. Jean Rochant Royal Marines 1st Brigade, on the main road
Capt. Guerinet Durand Picardy Musketeers 1st Brigade, on the main road

The record

Narrator — “General Masse arrays his forces. Rennes is small, but walled, and cannon have been allocated at key locations, forming a wall of fire to repel all who might seek to attack the main gate.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The General rides up and down the lines, shouting his plan for the first division to engage in a three-pronged assault. Nobody can quite hear or understand him, but his moustaches are rather inspiring. The result is 3, Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “Lt. General Auclair nods his understanding, and directs his brigades to move forwards.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The brigadier generals accept the straightforward order without question. The result is 3, Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “Bg. General Vasseur, eager for the 1st Brigade to distinguish itself, orders a bit greater speed of march. The 1st Brigade has the honor of taking the main road into town, and needs not concern itself with navigating uneven terrain, but has little cover to show for it.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “As the cannons open fire, the men of the Royal Marines and Picardy Musketeers show their mettle, and refuse to break. The result is 4, Inconclusive.”

Then the Narrator turned to the acting commander of the Royal Marines:

Narrator — “Marius, you have been ordered to advance on the gate in the face of withering cannon fire. Roll for your result.”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 1d6 — 1 (😮 😬)

Narrator — “Your men quail, but the iron discipline of the Marines shines through. You only need to slap a few men with the back of your cutlass to encourage them forward, even as their comrades are blown to pieces around them. The result is 4, Inconclusive.”

Narrator — “Col. Côté of the Picardy Musketeers, seeing an opportunity, rushes his regiment forward.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “Screaming and firing their muskets, the Musketeers successfully drive the defenders from the walls, leaving the city gate defenseless. The result is a 2, Success by storming party.” (👏 👏)

And then the finest foot commander the French Army has to offer spent his reputation in a single stroke:

Narrator — “The Musketeers tear the gates open. Desirous of glory, Vasseur charges his horse, first inside the walls. He finds himself alone and encircled by the terrified defenders. They pull the Bg. General from his horse and bayonet him without mercy as the horrified Musketeers try to rush to his aid.”

Narrator — “Marc Ducournau, Captain of the Royal Marines C Company, is one of the unlucky few struck by a rampaging cannonball. He does not survive his injuries.”

The city’s fate was pronounced the next afternoon, after the last personal rolls were in:

Narrator — “An uninspired army has overwhelmed an underwhelming defense. The city of Rennes has fallen. The conspirators are quickly clapped in irons, along with a few of their servants for good measure, and the lot are shipped off to Nantes to stand trial.”

The reckoning

Honors and the ledger

Rennes fell, the Chalais conspirators went to Nantes in irons, and every player character marched home. Marius Thibodeaux came back a brevet Colonel of the Royal Marines — commanding the regiment he had bought his way into as a captain only eight game-months before. Guerinet Durand was Mentioned in Despatches (+5 SP for three months, +1 per month thereafter) and carried off 1,400 crowns, the lion’s share of the plunder for being first through the gate without being bayoneted for it. Jean Rochant rolled snake eyes against Death and precisely nothing else — no mention, no promotion, no crowns — but honored the reverse suicide pact and lived. The butcher’s bill was borne by the NPCs: Bg. General Robert Vasseur, finest foot commander in the French Army, pulled from his horse and bayoneted alone inside the gates; and Capt. Marc Ducournau of the Royal Marines C Company, killed by a rampaging cannonball.

The morning after the resolution, Maj. Ladagnous volunteered the Picardy Musketeers 2nd Battalion — Guerinet included — straight into the Fall campaign:

Jean Rochant — “@DancingHorse Right back into the fray then?”

Marius Thibodeaux — “I imagine you’ll have a few sleepless nights on the front in the coming days”

Guerinet Durand — “Just when I was planning to use my newfound influence and wealth with the ladies!!”

And in the streets of Paris, the Marines came home:

Jean Rochant — “Marius Thibodeaux and I have returned from the frontlines and do we have stories to share! Of Mud! Of Blood! and of Glory! Who would care to join me in the first week of September at the Frog and Peach for carousing and stories to share?”

Marius Thibodeaux — “It’s been so long since I have set foot inside this city! grumbles as he pays back 200 crowns in loans“

Marius Thibodeaux — “My first week back, I have some important business to attend to.. my mistress must be quite lonely. I will be at the Frog and Peach the remaining three weeks though, drinking to being hale and hearty!”

Fall 1626 — FOR SOUBISE! The Ambush in the Cabbage Fields of Andorra

October–November 1626 · resolved at the table 16 April 2021 · Mission: Defence · Theater: the Principality of Andorra — the cabbage fields on the road to the capital

The situation

The fall season opened not with a war but with a peace. On the night of 16 April the Narrator posted The Fall Campaign (the table’s lone reaction: grim) and laid out the most delicate errand the gentlemen were ever handed:

Narrator — “Cardinal Richelieu himself has embarked on a secret mission to negotiate a lasting peace with Spain. Fearing English perfidy, he orders the Frontier Regiment to accompany him as an honor guard on his journey to the Principality of Andorra, where he will meet with the Habsburg representative to discuss final terms.”

The escort had been assembling for weeks of game time. The Picardy Musketeers were volunteered clear back in February (real time), the moment the Rennes campaign closed:

Jules Lavelle (GM) — “Picardy Musketeers 2nd Battalion Commander Maj. Augustin Ladagnous has decided to volunteer his unit for service in the Fall campaign. @DancingHorse will be deploying.”

Jean Rochant — “@DancingHorse Right back into the fray then?”

Marius Thibodeaux — “I imagine you’ll have a few sleepless nights on the front in the coming days”

Guerinet Durand — “Just when I was planning to use my newfound influence and wealth with the ladies!!”

In April the rest signed on. Marius volunteered his portion of the Royal Marines for November — “Cool, I volunteer my portion of the Royal Marines” (🚀 musket plank) — and in the Royal Foot Guards’ mess the officers talked themselves into it:

Hercule Bonaparte — “Well Gentlemen, we have been kept from the fields of glory for far too long. Let us go forth to remind our enemies what France’s elite fighting force truly looks like.”

Jules Lavelle — “Huzzah, back to the front where we belong, proving our mettle!”

Curtis Sinnoch — “I whole-heartily agree! For the honor and glory of France!” — and, days later: “To war!” … “WAR!”

Jean Rochant — “The rest of us are at war, correct?” — “Come to war with us!”

Days before the resolution — October 1626 in game — Jean Rochant paid 140 crowns for the Royal Marines’ open lieutenant-colonelcy (“I will pay!”). He would command the 1st Battalion for exactly one battle.

The order of battle

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiment is commanded by Col. Thierry Boulle, a steady leader whose lack of ambition or imagination is, perhaps, considered an asset by his Red Eminence for the purposes of this undertaking.”

Narrator — “Maj. Augustin Ladagnous has volunteered his Picardy Musketeers 2nd Battalion, including @DancingHorse, to serve under Col. Boulle.” (musket)

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards, commanded by @WillWorker, have also seen fit to tag along, perhaps to keep an eye on the Cardinal for the King.”

Narrator — “The Royal Marines, commanded by @etirflita, have attached themselves to the mission, presumably in case any rowboats need boarding.” (plank ×4)

Character Rank / post Unit
Hercule Bonaparte Colonel, commanding the regiment Royal Foot Guards
Curtis Sinnoch Lieutenant Colonel, 1st Battalion Royal Foot Guards
Jules Lavelle Brevet Major, commanding 2nd Battalion Royal Foot Guards
Marius Thibodeaux Colonel, commanding the regiment Royal Marines
Jean Rochant Lieutenant Colonel, commanding 1st Battalion Royal Marines
Guerinet Durand Captain, 2nd Battalion (under Maj. Ladagnous) Picardy Musketeers

And one more absence worth recording. Eighteen minutes into the session:

Narrator — “Dice Golem is down, so rolls will need to be made externally.”

Every roll of the deadliest night of the campaign war was made off-site, at rolldicewithfriends.com, on the honor system. The bot has an alibi.

The record

The fields go quiet

Narrator — “As French forces cross the border into Andorra, still several miles out from the capital city, they see nothing before them but fields full of toiling farmers. It is a clear day, the sun stands high in the sky, but the farmers keep at their labor.” (☀️ ☀️)

Narrator — “The Cardinal gives a muffled order from within his coach, and the train pulls to a stop. The assembled soldiery look around in confusion. The fields have gone unnaturally quiet.”

Narrator — “One of the farmers throws down his hoe and grabs an arquebus from where he’d hidden it amidst the cabbage. ‘FOR SOUBISE!’ he cries, and raises his weapon to fire.”

Narrator — “The other farmers similarly draw weapons to attack. The French forces are surrounded, and quickly drop down to defensive positions along the rows of vegetables.” (🍈)

Narrator — “This is a Defence campaign.”

Col. Boulle’s one good idea

Narrator — “Col. Boulle glances around in confusion, before raising his sword and roaring the order to FIRE!”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 for Boulle’s command — 6. “The battle result is 2 - Enemy broken by sortie.”

Narrator — “The order is heard clear, and the Frontier Regiment opens up on the attackers, who are cut down in droves, the fire withering them as surely as the sun will wither the untended vegetables.” (🥬)

The Marines wheel about

Narrator — “@etirflita, your forces wheel around madly, trying to make contact with the enemy up close. Roll for your battle result.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “1d6, n’est-ce pas?”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 2

Narrator — “Unused to maneuvers in a cabbage patch, your men scramble to arrange themselves in fighting order, and suffer losses before being able to return fire. The battle result is 4 - Inconclusive.”

The 1st Battalion in disarray

Narrator — “@ArcticFox, your 1st Battalion is in disarray and in need of your leadership. Your commander seems to have little sense of the situation at hand. Roll for your battle result.”

Jean Rochant — “1d6 for that, right?” — “Im new to command”

🎲 Jean Rochant rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 4

Narrator — “You follow Marius’s lead by not really leading at all, and there is nothing you can do to spare your men from the deadly ambush.” — “Your result is 4 - Inconclusive.”

The Cardinal’s syrupy voice

Narrator — “@WillWorker, the Cardinal’s syrupy voice drifts to you from within his coach, reminding you of the consequences of failure. Roll for your battle result.”

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 5

Narrator — “Hercule quickly arranges his men in staggered firing lines, and their muskets open up, tearing into the remaining English cowards. Your result is a 2 - Enemy broken by sortie.” (musket ×2)

“English cowards” — the first the table heard of who had actually armed the cabbage farmers. So much for fearing English perfidy; the Cardinal had feared exactly right.

The road to the city

Narrator — “@Jules Lavelle, your 2nd Battalion is tasked with traveling ahead to the city to ensure no further ambushes await.”

Jules Lavelle — “Be careful, men! We’ve survived the worst of the fighting, no reason to risk our lives any further. The English will die like dogs in due course either way.”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 1d6 for his battle result — 4

Narrator — “There are small pockets of resistance on the road, but you fight back bravely. Your result is a 3 - Inconclusive.”

Not a crooked soul remains

Honors and the ledger

The Cardinal reached his conference table unscratched, and the rewards poured out of his coach almost as fast as the bullets had poured into it. Hercule Bonaparte took a Mention (+6 SP), a 400-crown treasury note pressed into his hand by Richelieu personally, and the campaign’s grand prize: a brevet Brigadier-Generalcy and command of the Dragoon Brigade — a promotion he soon discovered was technically a step down in social precedence, prompting the Narrator’s ruling that “there are perks to being the king’s BFF.” Jules Lavelle, who had ridden into the guns on three points of reckless bravery, converted an unavailable promotion into his second Mention (+3 SP), took the Key to the City of Andorra (300 crowns) from the Prince himself, and rolled a 6 against a 5 to become Sir Jules Lavelle, Knight of France. Curtis Sinnoch — one point from death — likewise turned an unavailable promotion into a Mention (+2 SP) and collected 600 crowns in bounty for his battalion’s kill count, plus +1 MA for outperforming his commander. Guerinet Durand took his 250-crown share of the Frontier Regiment’s bonus home to Paris for the debauchery he had promised himself. Marius Thibodeaux survived, and got nothing else whatsoever.

And Jean Rochant — orphan, ex-private, cutlass-thrower, lieutenant-colonel of one month’s standing — got the finest death narration of the war and a place in the necrology. In the days that followed the succession was settled in the off-season channel:

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch, you are now the Acting Regimental Commander of the Royal Foot Guards.”

Hercule Bonaparte — “@Curtis Sinnoch Don’t redecorate the office just yet, but enjoy the chair. I don’t intend to be coming back, if it can be helped.” (plank ×2)

Curtis Sinnoch — “@WillWorker I’ll do my best - maybe bring in some fine cognac and restock the cabinet, but I’ll leave a furniture alone!”

Rochant’s gravestone was posted to the cemetery five days later. The image drew three 🎣 reactions — a fish out of water to the last.

Winter 1626–27 — Jersey: “The Oily Surface Ignites”

December 1626 – February 1627 · resolved at the table 11–12 July 2021 · Mission: Assault · Theater: the island of Jersey — the beaches, woods, and docks of Saint-Hélier

The situation

The winter of 1626–27 found France with no war to fight — and one insult it could not swallow. The Narrator laid out the strategic picture on the Sunday evening of the resolution:

Narrator — “It is a time of relative peace. The Spanish and Dutch are occupied with each other. The Savoyards and Genoese have learned their lesson, the Holy Roman Empire is riven with internal conflict, and the strength of the Huguenots has been broken.”

Narrator — “Only one foe threatens the security of France. From across the Channel, the heirs of Guillaume le Conquérant create trouble for God’s favored nation.” (three 😩 reactions)

Narrator — “Under the thinnest of pretexts, the English have seized certain French naval vessels tasked with patrolling coastal areas still rife with Huguenot sympathy.”

Narrator — “The King, furious, gives an ultimatum to the Cardinal. The Cardinal interprets the order, and makes it known to the Minister of War: there will be no official declaration of hostilities.”

Narrator — “Nevertheless, it is understood that some reaction is required, or else France will seem utterly weak. Still, no open conflict with English military forces can be permitted. The Minister settles on a softer target.”

The soft target was Jersey — an unofficial reprisal raid against a fishing town, by a strike force carrying seven player characters, the largest deployment of the war so far. The Narrator opened the evening with the campaign roll:

Narrator — “The Winter Campaign”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “This will be an Assault campaign.”

The order of battle

The deployment notice had gone out the night before: “The campaign will be resolved tomorrow evening. @WillWorker, @Curtis Sinnoch, @Jules Lavelle, @sirsfurther, @harry, @etirflita, and @ArcticFox will be deploying.” The Narrator read out the roster:

Narrator — “Taking the field:”

Narrator — “The Dragoon Brigade, commanded by @WillWorker, including Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons and the Princess Louisa Light Dragoons, itself commanded by @sirsfurther.”

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards, commanded by @Curtis Sinnoch, with @Jules Lavelle commanding the 1st Battalion.”

Narrator — “The Royal Marines, commanded by @etirflita, with @ArcticFox leading E Company.”

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiment, with @harry serving as a captain, and the Royal Northern Highlands Brigade.”

Narrator — “Col. Victor Charlet (MA 4) commands the Frontier Regiment this season.”

Character Post Where
Bg. Gen. (brevet) Hercule Bonaparte Commander, Dragoon Brigade the flanks
Robert d’Engaux Commander, Princess Louisa Light Dragoons the flanks
Curtis Sinnoch Commander, Royal Foot Guards the mile-long march inland
Sir Jules Lavelle 1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards forward scouting sortie
Lt. Col. Marius Thibodeaux Acting commander, Royal Marines amphibious assault on the docks
Jaques Peugeot E Company, Royal Marines amphibious assault on the docks
Capt. Clemens Charron Frontier Regiment, under Col. Charlet straight at the town

One more unit was on the island that night, though nobody knew it until the casualty lists were read: the Cardinal’s Guard.

The record

Narrator — “It is a frigid February morning as the French forces make their landing on the beaches of the island of Jersey. The city of Saint-Hélier stands just over a mile’s march inland.”

Narrator — “Early-morning fishermen spot the landing, and raise the alarm. The townspeople, confused, move to establish a hasty defense, but know little of the art of fortification. Their only hope lies in the confusion and disarray of the irregular volunteer forces coming against them.”

Narrator — “@WillWorker, the regiments under your command depart their boats with impressive speed, given the difficulty of controlling their mounts in the unfamiliar setting. Give your orders and roll.”

Hercule Bonaparte — “We have an impressive force for this assault. While the main armies approach from the front, the dragoons shall divide into two groups and, using their speed, move to compromise and demoralize our foe by position themselves at the enemy’s flanks.”

Narrator — “@sirsfurther, under Hercule’s orders, you sweep your regiment around and find yourself coming upon the surprised townsfolk from the flank. Your initial charge is a crushing success, but the townsfolk understand horse better than musket, and the fighting soon becomes fierce. Roll for your battle result.”

TO GLORY!”

The oil soaks into their uniforms, ensuring that even if they submerge themselves, they reignite as soon as they surface once more.” (🧯)

Aftermath in Paris

Robert d’Engaux — “I believe it was the distraction which my brave dragoons and I provided that led to this fantastic victory”

In the streets of Paris, the new brigadier-generals exchanged courtesies:

Hercule Bonaparte — “Dear Mr. @etirflita, A fellow Brigade General deserves to dine as one… at least once in a while. Please consider this an invitation to be my guest at Bothwell’s during the third week of March. Your fellow general, Sir Hercule Bonaparte”

Marius Thibodeaux — “@WillWorker, I would be delighted to attend. Civilised company will do wonders to help shake the dust of Jersey from my boots.”

And within hours of Jaques Peugeot’s fiery end, his player had rolled up a replacement — a noble second son with a 16 in Expertise — who arrived in the capital already dancing on the grave:

ArcticFox — “arrives in Paris, hot on the heels of the most recent battle. ‘I heard that fool second son of the Peugots went and got himself killed by jumping into an inferno. Their family will never learn!’”

ArcticFox — “I must buy the commander responsible a drink, as he has rid France of another turbulent Peugot!”

Marius Thibodeaux — “@ArcticFox, if you need an introduction at the Frog and Peach, my mistress and I will be there the last week of March. I would gladly accept a drink in exchange for your company. Maybe we could discuss filling out some newly vacant Royal Marine uniforms.. We colloquially refer to our troops as ‘red shirts’ due to the crimson we wear for bravery!”

Table talk

DancingHorse — “Oh, @sirsfurther , so naive” · “I’ll keep my fingers crossed for you” (the morning of the battle, to the campaign’s most eager volunteer)

etirflita — “Bah, the English can’t even win at football! Marius mutters before getting slaughtered in the campaign” · “Just kidding, @Dice Golem, you know I love you…” (🤣 — England had lost the Euro 2020 final that very evening)

Curtis Sinnoch — “(Awww come on, DG can’t do block rolls?)” (mid-fate-rolls, discovering Dice Golem’s limits)

DancingHorse — “So sorry @ArcticFox” · “You have been…flambéed”

ArcticFox — “C’est le Morte” · “I blame @etirflita” · “Maybe I’ll roll a noble finally” — and later that night: “I mena, Noble, second son, father was a knight who made BANK” · “Must be awesome at jousting” (👍)

Narrator — “@laiv.wire you have to die first” · laiv.wire — “I HAVE BEEN TRYING” (😠, on whether he could switch to a low-effort NPC role) · etirflita — “Challenge accepted!”

Jules Lavelle — “or new jersey. everything is legal in new jersey” · etirflita — “It’s okay, we burned Jersey to the ground last night.” · WillWorker — “Actually, that’s perfect. What better place for an illegal fight club than in the charred ruins of a conflagration?” · “(really that was just an excuse to use the word ‘conflagration’)”

etirflita — “You know what they say.. ‘Oily to bed, oily to rise, makes a man- oh God I’m on fire!!!’” (🤣🤣🤣)

❦

Spring 1627 — Chevreuse: The Campaign of Three Graves

March – May 1627 · resolved at the table 30 July 2021 · Mission: Assault · Theater: the town, fields, and château of Chevreuse, two days’ march from Paris

The situation

The enemy this spring was not across the Channel but in the very heartland — English gold and a hooded old nemesis stirring treason among the French nobility. The Narrator set the board:

Narrator — “The English are at it once more. The filthy sons of Albion spread their vile, corrupting touch across the very heartland of France. The English have sent the provacateur Walter Montagu to stir dissent among the nobility against the French monarchy. He has raised funds and commitments of soldiers to support a new force to be commanded by France’s old foe, the Duke of Soubise himself.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “Loyalists among the manor staff of Claude, Duke of Chevreuse (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lorrain.jpg), have reported to the Cardinal’s agents of a concentration of forces and supplies on his very lands a mere two days’ march from Paris.”

Narrator — “Chevreuse’s castle, the Château de la Madeleine, is a fortified hilltop redoubt with a military tradition over 500 years old. Despite its age, it has been consistently maintained and modernized, and the walls are reinforced to withstand cannonades.”

Narrator — “Although the castle is well-protected and will require a siege, the men and matériel intended for the war effort are scattered throughout the town and countryside of Chevreuse, and therefore vulnerable to Assault.”

Narrator — “The Royal forces arrive from the north-east. There will be fighting in both town and field, to be sure. The enemy was alerted shortly after the army left Paris, and has lined the hills and fields with hastily-assembled earthworks and their accompanying trenches. The town of Chevreuse, too, has been fortified, with blockades and traps lining the streets, ready to harass and slow any advancing force.”

The order of battle

The muster had been arranged days before, in a burst of voluntelling. Henri Toulouse had already gone ahead — “@laiv.wire sets off for the front lines, signing on with the Frontier Regiment,” the Narrator noted on 19 July — and Hercule set the tone for everyone else:

Hercule Bonaparte — “May orders: Hercule voluntells the Dragoons into the spring campaign.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “What’s left of the Royal Marines will be by your side!”

Hercule Bonaparte — “You’re Brevet BG of the 1st Brigade, remember? You also have the Picardy Musketeers in your collection.” (👀)

Marius Thibodeaux — “Hell, why not. Let’s make it a party- Picardy Musketeers as well!” (🎊)

Hercule Bonaparte — “Hopefully @DancingHorse and @sirsfurther are recovered from their injuries by the time we get to the front.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “What they need is some battlefield camaraderie to heal those wounded hearts.” (❤️‍🩹)

Robert d’Engaux — “‘It was only a scratch’ wheezes Robert”

Marius Thibodeaux — “I’ve never steered anyone under my command wrong, you’ll be just fine under my wing, @sirsfurther! cough-“

Hercule Bonaparte — “I believe that should be directed at @DancingHorse. @sirsfurther is a Dragoon of exceptional promise.”

Pépin Bonheur — “I am prepared to do my duty” (🧨)

On the morning of the resolution Jean Renault rolled to see whether his regiment would be committed — 🎲 1d6 — 4 — and made the best of it:

Jean Renault — “I have been monitoring my men for this past month after being given my command, and the Crown Prince Cuirassiers are ready to fight again for France!!” · “The Crown Prince Cuirassiers volunteer!”

The Narrator read the final roster:

Narrator — “Deploying: -@WillWorker’s Dragoon Brigade, including @sirsfurther in Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons -@etirflita’s 1st Brigade of Foot, including @DancingHorse in the Picardy Musketeers -@Curtis Sinnoch’s Royal Foot Guards, including @Jules Lavelle -@ArcticFox’s Crown Prince Cuirassiers, 3rd Squadron -@laiv.wire’s 53rd Fusiliers, 3rd Battalion (acting)

And assorted other squadrons/battalions.”

In character terms: Bg. Gen. Hercule Bonaparte led the Dragoon Brigade, with Robert d’Engaux commanding the 1st Squadron of Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons under Col. Martin Breguet; Bvt. Bg. Gen. Marius Thibodeaux led the 1st Brigade of Foot — the remnant Royal Marines and the Picardy Musketeers of Col. Sylvestre Côté, with Pépin Bonheur in the ranks; Col. Curtis Sinnoch led the Royal Foot Guards, with Jules Lavelle at the head of the 1st Battalion; Maj. Jean Renault rode with the Crown Prince Cuirassiers’ 3rd Squadron; and Cpt. Henri Toulouse held acting command of the 53rd Fusiliers’ 3rd Battalion.

Narrator — “The top commanding officers on the field, @WillWorker, @etirflita, @Curtis Sinnoch, @ArcticFox, and @laiv.wire must array their forces, give their orders, and roll.”

The record

Marius Thibodeaux — “Wary of traps, the 1st Brigade of Foot will slowly and systematically clear the field in an attempt to recover any matériel intended for the war effort and scout for any saboteurs!”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 1d6 — 2 (😓×2)

Narrator — “The fields are relatively quiet, and little materiel is discovered. A series of saboteurs are uncovered and dealt with, but not before managing to blow up a few companies. Your result is Inconclusive (BR 4).” (💥×2)

Narrator — “The Picardy Musketeers are at the vanguard, as there are too few Royal Marines to fill a front line.”

Narrator — “Col. Sylvestre Côté sees an opportunity, spying an unguarded munitions cache in the middle of a quiet grove. He orders his men forward to seize it.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “The regiment’s scouts are the first in, and the first to lay hands on the munitions, which, of course, have been trapped. The munitions are destroyed, with only two lives lost. A Bloody victory (BR 2).”

Those two scouts, and that trapped cache, would matter very much to Pépin Bonheur in a few minutes.

Jean Renault — “The Crown Prince Cuirassiers will use their superior speed and agility to harass the enemies as they find them.”

Narrator — “@ArcticFox, you only command the 3rd Squadron. Roll when ready.”

🎲 Jean Renault rolls 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “New to cavalry command, Jean orders his riders to sweep through the lightly-forested countryside for foes. Not lightly enough, as the trees turn out to be littered with traitors, who fire down into the unsuspecting horsemen, or just drop rocks on their heads. Dozens of men are slain. Your result is Repulsed (BR 5).” (😖, plank)

Curtis Sinnoch — “My men! Once again we are beset by the vile English upon our very shores! Do we allow them to spread their filth upon our land? Or, like the snake, do we cut off the head and allow its body to die! To the Estate! Spare non with sympathies to the foul English! @Jules Lavelle - you have full command of your regimen to scout and act as you see fit. For the honor of France!”

Jules Lavelle — “For France and Glory!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards advance slowly and surely towards the town. The fighting is fierce, the town well defended. but Chevreuse always expected its castle to weather attacks, and the steadfast training of the Guards overwhelms the poorly-drilled traitor detritus Soubise has so far assembled. The fighting is hard, but the result undeniable. Curtis enjoys a Bloody victory (BR 2).”

Then came the orders that would define the campaign — and end an era. Hercule Bonaparte, first rolled in September 1624 and by now a Knight and Brigadier-General, gave the Dragoon Brigade its mission:

Hercule Bonaparte — “Taking inspiration from their opponent’s in the previous campaign, Hercule inspects the Dragoons personally before they depart France. In particular, the specialized incendiaries each soldier has been issued. There is only one thing to do with English who invade our lands and those who tolerate their presence: burn the cancer out. Hercule instructs his forces that for this assault, the Dragoons will be adopting the role of the Chevauchée. Using their speed, they will swing wide from the main front to approach from the west, where they will push until they encounter halting resistance. At that point, from their halted position, they will set fire to everything and let the purifying flames conduct the rest of their grim work. With luck, the conflagration may even threaten the castle.” (🏇×2, 🧨)

🎲 Hercule Bonaparte rolls 1d6 — 5 (🔥)

Narrator — “It is hard riding, but the Dragoon Brigade’s main force keeps clear of overhanging branches, and most of them survive the maneuver to enact their commander’s plan. As the bulk of the resistance is positioned to the east, none but the peasantry remain to raise the alarm, ignored, as their homes and livelihoods go up in flames. Hercule enjoys a Bloody victory (BR 2).” (🔥×2, 🥳×3)

Narrator — “Col. Martin Breguet, leading Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons, pushes forward into the town, unconcerned by the flames.”

🎲 Narrator rolls 1d6 — 1 (😢)

Narrator — “His horses balk, carrying their roaring riders out of the battle. The result is Inconclusive (BR 4).”

Narrator — “@sirsfurther, you may try to save the day for your 1st Squadron. Give your orders and roll 1d6”

While the town burned, its burner watched:

Hercule Bonaparte — “Hercule dispassionately gazes at the inferno. It is a harsh message, but one which must be sent. Those who stand idle in the face of treason, become traitors themselves. Perhaps there will be less idleness the next time the English try to get a toehold in France and Hercule’s presence will not be required.”

Robert d’Engaux — “Robert has spent the morning studying maps of the area in the hopes of finding somewhere full of treasure. He eventually finds the house of a local merchant. He orders his squadron to slip away from the main force to take the house”

🎲 Robert d’Engaux rolls 1d6 — 6 (👏)

Narrator — “@sirsfurther, the merchant is the wealthiest man in town. So wealthy, in fact, that he has hired his own guards to protect against the rowdy and corrupt traitor army gathering in his town. The well-trained mercenaries open fire on your men at perfect range. The numbers are on your side, but your men came unprepared for battle, and are cut down in droves before they overcome the mercenaries in bloody hand-to-hand combat. Is loot worth this cost? Your result is Pyrrhic victory (BR 3).” (😢)

Robert d’Engaux — “Less to share with”

Jules Lavelle — “My men shall clear the side streets of any lurking traitors. None shall take us unawares!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle rolls 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “The side streets are exactly where the defending forces have arrayed themselves, and the Foot Guards 1st Battalion marches into the thick of it. Although they avoid the traps of the main avenue, the fighting is fierce and terrible, made worse by the orange glow of doom fast approaching from the west side of town, and the smells of burning wood and flesh. Dozens of men fall, but the traitors cannot hold back the spirit of the Royal Foot Guards. The result is Pyrrhic victory (BR 3).”

Last to move was the acting battalion commander of the 53rd, an hour behind the others:

Henri Toulouse — “With the rising ash and smoke, Cpt. Toulouse and his troops stand ready to surround what little remains of the smouldering gates and crush the fleeing survivors, terrible fusil at the ready.”

🎲 Henri Toulouse rolls 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Survivors pour forward from the gates, but few of them appear to be soldiers. Nevertheless, Henri’s men unleash fusillade after fusillade into the unlucky townspeople, leaving a corpse pile impressively tall. Your result is Inconclusive (BR 4).”

Henri Toulouse — “Ah, zut.”

The reckoning

Pépin Bonheur — the twirling two-by-four

Narrator — “@DancingHorse, your rolls are Death 9, Mention 7, Promotion 8, Crowns 4”

Pépin Bonheur — “No modifiers for me: death”

Narrator — “Survived!”

🎲 Pépin Bonheur rolls 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “oh, yes” · “so that’s death” · “survived anyway”

It hadn’t been survived at all. The two stray d6s were, in fact, the two halves of one Death roll:

Narrator — “wait” · “you didn’t survive” (😱) · “didn’t realize you rolled a 2nd 1d6”

Narrator — “so we’re adding those I guess”

Narrator — “Pépin wisely stands far back from the obvious trap. As fragments of teeth and bones of the two scouts rain down on the assembled regiment, he shields his eyes from the downpour of debris. Had he kept them uncovered, he may have seen the smoldering, twirling two-by-four, somehow intact after the explosion, falling down towards where he stands. It whizzes past, but on the backturn makes solid contact with his skull, and Pépin’s battle, and life, are over.” (plank ×6, 😓)

Marius Thibodeaux — a general at last

Hercule Bonaparte — the hooded Duke

Narrator — “As Hercule watches the fires burn, the glint in his eyes seems otherworldly. The burning horses running back towards him do nothing to stir the man with ice in his veins. Alongside the fleeing Light Dragoons, one strange rider goes unnoticed, tearing out of the flames. Hercule spots something strange about the man, and raises his sword. The hooded Duke of Soubise draws a pistol and fires a ball straight into the Brigadier-General’s chest. Hercule falls from his horse without a sound, and the Duke flees into the night. The creeping flames overtake the noble Knight, and nothing remains but a dream of ambition.” (grim ×2, 🧐, plank)
