She seems unharmed, but drinks a glass of wine and falls dead asleep.

Henri focuses on paperwork, after-action reports and death notices. He remains so engrossed that he doesn’t notice the girl awaken a couple hours later. She slips out of the bedroll, reaches for a shaving razor, and gives Henri the last shave he will ever receive.” (grim ×2, 🪒)

Henri Toulouse — “Finally, the warm touch of a belle chere…”

The aftermath

Narrator — “The Spring Campaign has concluded. Another town burned, another victory for France.” (🇫🇷)

Narrator — “The castle surrenders immediately after the town is taken, without need for a siege. The Duke of Chevreuse successfully pins the blame on his wife, Marie de Rohan, claiming she went behind his back to work with Walter Montagu and Soubise. To the great fury of the Cardinal, Soubise is nowhere to be found in castle or town.”

Between 6:09 and 6:17 that evening, the Narrator posted three headstones to the cemetery channel, one after another — Pépin Bonheur (☁️×2), Hercule Bonaparte (💍×2), Henri Toulouse (🥷×2). Three player characters dead in a single victorious afternoon; no campaign of the war had cost more. Back in Paris, the survivors organized the wakes:

Robert d’Engaux — “Upon his return from campaign, Robert writes to @ArcticFox and @laiv.wire to invite them to toady at Red Phillips on the 1st week of the next month”

Jean Renault — “I will happily celebrate with you then! However in turn, the second week, the officers of the horse will be toasting to those who fell on the second week! Would you, @sirsfurther , @etirflita @Jules Lavelle, @laiv.wire, or anyone else want to join us in the celebration of life? We will curse the traitorous Duke’s name!” (🥂)

Jules Lavelle — “Thank you, but I do believe Hunter’s will be more than enough for me.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “Empty Chairs at Empty Tables starts playing in Marius’ head”

Curtis Sinnoch — “It is with great sorrow I shall drink to the memory of good @WillWorker - thought we had not always seen eye to eye, we both came through the rank’s of the Kings Footmen and I shall fondly remember his acts for the glory of France. I care not for standard social graces - @everyone who fought in that damned skirmish, I will be happy to host at Bothwell’s at the end of the month, and I’ll foot the bill. Tis the only fitting way to pay our respects for a fallen Brigadier-General.” · “Indeed! Though Hercule may have been the first, he will certainly not be the last”

Jules Lavelle — “When he receives the news about Bg. Gen. Bonaparte, Jules Lavelle appears visibily deflated.”

Table talk

sirsfurther — “What happens if I don’t pay back my debt?” · etirflita — “If you die, you’re golden. Debt free” (👼) · ArcticFox — “Use one credit card to pay the others!”

ArcticFox — “Be EXTRA Brave” · etirflita — “RECKLESSLY so” · “It’s Spring forward, Fall back, right? Charge!” (an hour later, Reckless Bravery 1 had killed Hercule)

sirsfurther — “I was hoping to rifle through @DancingHorse ‘s wallet, but he chickened out” · DancingHorse — “I have a family!! Younger brothers and all that” · etirflita — “*had…” · “Sorry- I’m trying to keep us safe! haha”

WillWorker — “What a run.” · “Fire is a fickle mistress. Hopefully next character I’m not hamstrung with 30 Endurance. Have yet to actually duel.” (😭×2) · “Sept of 1624 was when he was rolled” (😖×2) · “But I shall defer rolling it for a while and use the grief of the loss as an excuse to drink.”

Jules Lavelle — “NORMALLY I would be more offended but considering you just lost the best character in the game, I’ll let you lash out” · DancingHorse — “Seriously though, Hercule was the BEST character so far. He did the most intense social climbing.” · Curtis Sinnoch — “I would argue, but it was lonely at the top, starting at SL11”

etirflita — “Like ‘if I roll 2d6 and get a high number I will survive?’ No. You are dead, time to make a new character” (grim) · WillWorker — “It teaches valuable lessons, such as "3% happens".”

Narrator — “@gentlemen We are down to 7 active players, which is on the low side for interesting/dynamic play. Please think of any friends you may know who might be interested in playing and consider inviting them to check the server out.” (the three graves cost the table dearly, too — laiv.wire and harry went inactive that same evening)

❦

Summer 1627 — The Flaming Galleon of Île de Ré

June – August 1627 · resolved at the table 21–23 August 2021 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: Île de Ré, off the Atlantic coast of France

The situation

Two years after the Frontier Regiments stormed its fortress, Île de Ré was again the hinge of the war — this time with the Duke of Buckingham himself standing on it. On the evening of 21 August the Narrator opened the summer books:

Narrator — “The summer heat will stir even the most languid foe into open conflict with the right-hearted favorites of God. The army is once again deployed to the fortress-island of Ré. The English, dissatisfied with the progress in the Huguenot rebellion they’ve so vigorously encouraged, and seeking to lift the siege on La Rochelle by any means necessary, have landed several thousand men on the Île de Ré. With the Duke of Buckingham himself commanding the English forces, they have been able to swiftly cover the grounds of the island generally, with only the star fort keeping them at bay. Buckingham has settled in for a siege, and only brave smugglers, incentivized by wise bounties from Cardinal Richelieu, have been able to keep the fortress supplied.”

Narrator — “The army arrives as part of a rescue mission, seeking to break the siege and save the courageous defenders of Saint-Martin-de-Ré. The full might of the Royal Army crashing onto the shores ought to shatter the morale of any enemy, but Buckingham orders what defenders he can to go to ground, spreading themselves over the island to fight man-to-man where necessary.”

Narrator — “All units are performing Field Operations.”

In the OOC channel that same night, the mood was set in one line:

etirflita — “We’re going to make the English go down so hard!”

The order of battle

Narrator — “Royal forces are organized as one enormous army, commanded by Gen. Serge Vaganay, regarded as the most dynamic and brilliant military mind ever to lead a French army in battle.”

Narrator — “Beneath Gen. Vaganay, forces are arrayed as follows: [order-of-battle charts attached] As well as the Frontier Division and Regiment.”

Narrator — “The general’s adjutants produce a hastily-drawn map of the island and the known disposition of English forces.”

The NPC commanders, introduced in the Narrator’s own words as the battle unfolded:

  • Gen. Serge Vaganay — army commander, “regarded as the most dynamic and brilliant military mind ever to lead a French army in battle.”
  • Lt. Gen. Bernard Lefebvre — 1st Division (reserve), “a distant cousin of @WillWorker [Private Sébastien Lefebvre], has earned great respect for his sober, direct leadership and absolute refusal to surrender.”
  • Bg. Gen. Frank Bouchard — Guards Brigade.
  • Lt. Gen. Jérémie De Michele — Cavalry Division, “a young man of illustrious lineage.”
  • Bg. Gen. Denis Nee — Horse Guards Brigade.
  • Bg. Gen. Jean-Martin Desjardins — Heavy Brigade.
  • Col. Gaël Roquebert — Crown Prince Cuirassiers, “a man of duty and honor.”
  • Col. Martin Breguet — Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons, acting commander of the Dragoon Brigade, “although inexperienced as a general officer, nevertheless possesses one of the keenest military minds the Dragoons have ever produced.”

The player characters in the line of battle:

Character Rank / Post Station
Marius Thibodeaux Brigadier General, 1st Brigade of Foot 1st Division, in reserve at the landing site
Curtis Sinnoch Colonel, Royal Foot Guards Guards Brigade, guarding the landing site
Jules Lavelle Major, 1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards Guards Brigade, under Col. Sinnoch
Jean Renault Major, Crown Prince Cuirassiers Heavy Brigade, Cavalry Division
Robert d’Engaux Brevet Lt. Colonel, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons Dragoon Brigade, under Col. Breguet

The record

Narrator — “Gen. Vaganay has tussled with Buckingham before. He surveys the map, and compares it to the marshy ground stretching before him. It’s mid-morning before the Royal forces are finished disembarking and sufficiently organized to begin the attack. The explosions of cannon echo over the waves from the sea battle taking place just off the island.”

Narrator — “If Saint-Martin is to be saved, it will require all his arts. He summons his division commanders. The 1st Division will stay in reserve, to reinforce where needed. The 2nd Division will make up the main attack force, spreading out to search and destroy the enemy. The Cavalry Division will range ahead, spotting and engaging only where necessary. The Dragoon Brigade will be sent in as a surgical strike force, seeking to circle around and cut off the English forces in the field from their command camp by the siege zone.”

🎲 Gen. Vaganay’s command roll: 1d6 — 2

Narrator — “The orders are textbook. The battlefield is not. The cavalry finds itself terribly slowed by the marshy ground. Without their intelligence, the infantry are set upon from all sides as they advance, though at least they come expecting it. The result is Inconclusive (BR 4).”

Narrator — “Lt. Gen. Bernard Lefebvre, a distant cousin of @WillWorker, has earned great respect for his sober, direct leadership and absolute refusal to surrender. He deploys his infantry to cover the rear with great precision and care, setting up a defensive perimeter around the landing zone designed to be completely impenetrable to enemy spies and saboteurs.”

🎲 Lt. Gen. Lefebvre: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Just in time, as the final hastily-assembled fencing goes up, a squadron of English dragoons attempt to slip through the picket. Unseen ropes tangle their horses’ legs, and they go crashing to the ground, where they are easily set upon and slaughtered. Their comrades, watching from a distance, turn and run, leaving the landing site unmolested. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Narrator — “As he watches the enemy dragoons flee, Bg. Gen. Frank Bouchard, commanding the Guards Brigade, feels a tap on his shoulder. His adjutant has noticed some civilians wandering towards their lines, and is asking what they should do.”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Bouchard: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “Bg. Gen. Bouchard orders his men to ignore the civilians. His adjutant, feeling suspicious, approaches to inspect them anyway. The seemingly ragtag group throws aside their cloaks to reveal they’ve come packing pistols. They begin firing into the astonished Guards, but the adjutant raises his own rapier skewers several, taking a musket ball for his trouble. The Guards are left mostly intact thanks to the adjutant’s quick thinking. The result is Inconclusive (BR 4).”

Then the image the campaign would be remembered by:

Narrator — “Col. @Curtis Sinnoch’s Royal Foot Guards stand watch as the lower-ranking regiments clean up the mess from the civilian attack. He notices the sounds of the sea battle getting closer. Then, without further warning, an English galleon appears from behind a stony cliff, its decks aflame, bearing down directly on the landing site. Crazed English sailors, resigned to their fate, fire muskets from the rigging down into the assembled Guards. Col. Sinnoch, declare your orders and roll!”

Narrator — “As the Guards Brigade deals with emergencies from behind the lines, the 1st Brigade of Foot under Bg. Gen. @etirflita prepares to reinforce units in the field. Rather than wait for calls, the regimental commanders request to be set loose to maneuver, actively seeking out opportunities to reinforce, rather than risk being too late. Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux, declare your orders and roll!”

Bg. Gen. Marius Thibodeaux — “The 1st Brigade of Foot will hold position awaiting calls for reinforcement in order to be deployed in a strategic manner. We will not split into guerrilla forces so a few commanders can gain glory at the expense of my men’s lives.”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “Your orders prove prescient; not a single call for reinforcements comes, except from the Guards Brigade stationed behind you. The landing site would’ve been completely overrun, the invasion force stranded, but for your wise forbearance. Your result is Enemy crushed (BR 1).”

Narrator — “Cavalry Division commander Lt. Gen. Jérémie De Michele, a young man of illustrious lineage, orders his brigadier generals to approach the scouting mission as though they were scouting attackers on Paris itself. Though the wet ground presents issues for the horses, thoroughness is considered more important than speed.”

🎲 Lt. Gen. De Michele: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Gen. Vaganay’s subordinates are proving themselves much more adept than their commander. The enemy are quickly spotted and targeted for destruction, with the cavalry avoiding direct conflict and following the plan of battle impeccably. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Narrator — “Horse Guards commander Bg. Gen. Denis Nee must choose between keeping his regiments near the shore, or pressing inland. He selects the shore, leaving the inland for the Heavy Brigade.”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Nee: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The enemy have avoided the shore almost entirely, pressing inland to lay their traps, either to avoid bombardment or make it harder to spot them. No matter the why, little glory accrues to the Horse Guards. The result is Inconclusive (BR 3).”

Narrator — “The Heavy Brigade, under Bg. Gen. Jean-Martin Desjardins, performs the needed scouting inland. The island is criss-crossed with small streams, rocky outcroppings, and woods, making it truly treacherous terrain for cavalry. Desjardins has been ordered against speed, but his men and horses are agitated at the unwelcoming site of battle.”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Desjardins: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “The increased pace proves essential for the outcome of the battle. The Heavy Brigade encounters an entire enemy division marching double-time to reach pre-built defenses, and delivers their location with speed enough to avoid the 2nd Brigade marching straight into the death trap. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Narrator — “Col. Gaël Roquebert of the Crown Prince Cuirassiers, a man of duty and honor, follows the order to disengage once the enemy division has been spotted. Even so, during the retreat, an advance force of English cuirassiers comes sweeping into their flanks. Col. Roquebert frantically orders his troops to wheel around and battle for their lives.”

🎲 Col. Roquebert: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “The men and horse prove themselves disciplined, and the good order that characterizes the attack permeates the performance of the regiment. They make quick work of the starved, howling English. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Narrator — “Maj. @ArcticFox of the Crown Prince Cuirassiers finds his squadron separated from the rest of the force by a single Troop of enemy cavalry. The crazed captain shouts something in his ugly Germanic tongue. He seems to be challenging Jean to, could it be, a joust? Jean, give your orders and roll.”

Jean’s answer would have to wait until the end of the battle — the table moved on to the Dragoons.

Narrator — “The Dragoon Brigade, temporarily commanded by Col. Martin Breguet of Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons, attempts to slip past enemy patrols to find its way between the siege and the enemy force proper. Breguet, although inexperienced as a general officer, nevertheless possesses one of the keenest military minds the Dragoons have ever produced.”

🎲 Col. Breguet: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The plan fails; the Dragoons are spotted before they can even get close to the siege zone. But Breguet’s expertise in regimental command enables him to organize a fighting withdrawal that averts disaster for his men. The result is Inconclusive (BR 3).”

Narrator — “With Col. Breguet running the brigade, it falls to Brevet Lt. Colonel @sirsfurther to lead Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons in battle. The alarm has been called, and hordes of enemy soldiers are pouring towards your embattled line. As Col. Breguet screams for you to disengage, your captains insist that these filthy English can be beaten, if only you can find the courage to stand and fight. Robert, give your orders and roll!”

Robert d’Engaux — “Men! These roast beef protestants are nothing to be afraid of! Let us show them the fiercesomeness of fighting Frenchman!”

Robert d’Engaux — “Robert calculates that the English are just far enough away for his dragoons to dismount and fire two volleys before remounting and charging directly into the centre of their line.”

🎲 Robert d’Engaux: 1d6 — 4

Meanwhile, on the beach, Colonel Sinnoch answered the burning ship:

Col. Curtis Sinnoch — “Solider of the Royal Foot Guard! See how this ship is full of dead men - they just don’t know it yet! They will come with reckless yet fierce abandon - cover your brothers in arms, keep your head down, and allow this foe to be delivered to God himself! FIRE!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch: 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “@sirsfurther’s orders are carried out perfectly, and his men even manage a full three volleys, sending the mad English reeling. Their charge, however, is met with surprising readiness by another fresh line behind the initial wave, who repel the dragoons in short order. The withdrawal continues as planned. The result is Inconclusive (BR 3).”

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch’s Royal Foot Guard arrange themselves behind whatever cover the landing site offers. They return fire at the doomed English sailors, but before long, even the cannons of the flaming galleon begin to roar, scattering men and materiél across the shoreline. All seems lost until the disciplined volleys of the RFG land a lucky shot on the ship’s pilot, who slumps against the wheel. The enemy vessel turns sharply, and instead of its inertia carrying it onto the beach, it falls onto its side in the shallows. English sailors are sent careening into the tide, the flames of their fury falling fruitlessly into the sea, to be shot dead by the Guard before they can rise again. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Narrator — “Maj. @Jules Lavelle takes the initiative, ordering his 1st Battalion to wade into the water to finish off any survivors.”

Maj. Jules Lavelle — “My family used to summer on the Île de Ré. This is no different from spearfishing my dinner!”

🎲 Jules Lavelle: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “The burned, battered, battle-weary English have no chance against the fresh French. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Col. Curtis Sinnoch — “Well Done @Jules Lavelle ! Watch your head, and watch for some trinkets to remind yourself of this fine day!”

The reckoning

Marius Thibodeaux — knighted on the sand

Narrator — “@etirflita, gain +1 MA for outperforming your commander in this battle.”

Narrator — “@etirflita, your rolls are Death 14, Mention 9, Promotion 8, Crowns 3”

Marius declared 1 reckless bravery.

Narrator — “Your colonels bring you 400 crowns’ worth of loot in captured enemy gold medals.”

Narrator — “For your exemplary performance in battle, the King is considering granting you a title. Roll 1d6. Due to your prior consideration, you need only a 4.”

Narrator — “Rise, Sir Marius.”

Robert d’Engaux — “Congratulations, sir”

The battle decided — and the man the Narrator forgot

Narrator — “Buckingham’s field army has been shattered, but the siege camp maintains its defensive perimeter. With the sea battle being decided in favor of the French as well, it’s only a matter of time before the English are driven from the island. Still, a portion of their forces remain strong and defensive, a siege within a siege.”

Sir Marius Thibodeaux — “Hopefully @ArcticFox is victorious against this remnant”

Narrator — “Knew I was forgetting someone” — “He won’t be resolving until tomorrow”

Jean Renault — the joust declined, and the cross of Lancelot du Lac

Past midnight the next day, Jean finally answered the crazed English captain’s challenge (the GM ran this resolution from his player account):

Jean Renault — “These men are not known for their honor, but we will show them what France is made of!”

Jean Renault — “I yell back in English, ‘A joust? Surely you jest!’”

Jean Renault — “Turn your horses around, go home, or be buried here far from your lands!”

Narrator — “The enemy troop charges, though you double their number. Roll 1d6”

🎲 Jean Renault: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “They never stand a chance. Your men let them close the distance before bringing the hammer down, trapping the enemy between two ordered lines. The result is Enemy driven from field (BR 2).”

Jean Renault — “I tried to warn them”

Narrator — “Jean, your rolls are Death 13, Mention 9, Promotion 8, Crowns 7”

🎲 2d6 death — 6 (survives) 🎲 2d6 mention — 5 (no mention) 🎲 2d6 promotion — 7 (no promotion) 🎲 2d6 crowns — 7, then 2d6 loot — 5

Narrator — “The captain who challenged you wore an ancestral diamond-studded chivalric cross worth 500 crowns. the name ‘Lancelot du Lac’ is engraved on the back.”

Jean Renault — “Sacre Bleu!”

Honors, plunder, and the aftermath in Paris

Not one player character died on the Île de Ré — the first campaign in recent memory to add no new tombstones. The season’s ledger: Sir Marius Thibodeaux knighted, mentioned in dispatches, +1 MA, and 400 crowns in captured gold medals; Curtis Sinnoch created Baron, +1 MA, and 500 crowns; Robert d’Engaux confirmed as a full Lieutenant Colonel; Jules Lavelle mentioned in dispatches (+1 SP forever) and 400 crowns richer; Jean Renault carrying home a 500-crown relic he would refuse to ever sell.

Back in Paris, the veterans met in the streets to compare stories:

Jean Renault — “I got separated from the main forces, did everyone make it home alive from this summer’s campaign?”

Jules Lavelle — “Of course. The English are a laughable foe. Though they did fight with courage, I will admit.”

Jean Renault — “Indeed, to the point of insanity I may add” — “My men and I thoroughly thrashed them”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Indeed @Jules Lavelle - they can fight with courage. They are also foolhardy and dim-witted, and expert fire from our regiment drove them from the glorious field! Your men in particular did an admirable job ensuring none of the British made it to the surf - excellent marksmanship.”

Jules Lavelle — “Thank you, sir. It was easier than stabbing bilge rats in beggar’s alley back home.”

Primo Tremblay — “Mon Dieu, Paris can’t talk about anything but the War! I must find my way into a better regiment if I’m to have any hope of securing my family’s future!”

Sir Marius Thibodeaux — “The Royal Marines are always looking for fresh meat.. I mean, fresh men!”

In the off-season channel, the recruiting mills also turned. Sébastien Lefebvre — the Lieutenant General’s distant cousin — finally passed muster with the Gascon Regiment on a roll of 5:

Narrator — “On paper, Sebastien appears to be the perfect candidate; he is not yet deceased. His name is shouted in the street, with the rest of the Regiment’s recruits, as part of the standard process of informing next-of-kin of imminent funeral proceedings.”

Appointments followed: Sir Marius elected Maj. Augustin Ladagnous of the Picardy Musketeers as his Brigade Major. The fall volunteer rolls claimed Jerome Greybeck, whose battalion commander volunteered on a 6:

Jerome Greybeck — “Jerome, sure a big win was just around the corner, is dismayed to put his gambling hobby on hold. However, duty calls. Perhaps on his return he will have something more worthwhile to wager.”

Sir Marius Thibodeaux — “Instead, for the next few months, Jerome will be gambling his life.”

And the new Baron Sinnoch went title-shopping. On 23 August he stood for the vacant ministry:

Baron Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah, another resounding victory for us! I think I may investigate government work - my father suggested that it was fitting of my new title, though I find it dreadfully boring. I’ll be submitting myself as a candidate as the new Minister of Justice - the previous monsieur evidently ran afoul of the church and has fled the country. As it happens, Felice’s father has some contacts with the Minister of state, I wonder if he can assist me?”

🎲 1d6 (needed a 5) — 6 — Narrator: “Congratulations to the new Minister of Justice.”

🎲 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “Cardinal Richelieu notes the rising Minister Sinnoch’s career with great interest. Words are whispered. Deals are arranged. Curtis Sinnoch has been made a Marquis.”

Table talk

etirflita — “Haha jinxed! @ArcticFox hasn’t rolled” — “The death tax must be paid.”

❦

Fall 1627 — The Battle of the Scarecrows

September – November 1627 · resolved at the table 28 September – 4 October 2021 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: the Aquitaine farmland around besieged La Rochelle

The situation

The Marquis-Colonel of the Royal Foot Guards announced the season himself, a week before the books opened:

Curtis Sinnoch — “Fine Gentlemen of Paris! Spend an extra night with your mistresses, and all Subalterns are excused of duty! (The Privates will cover for anything necessary). WE GO TO WAR THIS MONTH!”

On 28 September the Narrator rolled a 6 for the campaign — “This will be a Field Operations campaign” — and laid out the treachery at La Rochelle:

Narrator — “After suffering miserable defeat under the leadership of Duke Soubise, the city of La Rochelle was thought to have learned its lesson, and become an ardent bastion of the Royalists. However, under pressure from the vile Buckingham and his fleet’s blockade, and the besieging Huguenot army, the city’s masters have broken down, opening the gates to let in Buckingham and his cohort.

Cardinal Richelieu himself has come to direct the siege efforts. With the bulk of the regular army devoted to the siege, the Cardinal has directed the Minister of War to allocate certain regiments to support the Frontier Regiment in scouring the countryside for lingering Huguenot forces bent on breaking the siege.”

Narrator — “Although the Huguenots are scattered across the landscape, they are currently well-supplied and moving in cohesive military units. With their leadership holed up in La Rochelle, the rebels’ hopes lie entirely on the English-supported field units meeting the Royal Army in open battle and emerging victorious to lift the siege.”

Narrator — “Royalist forces will be attempting to seek-and-destroy the enemy over eighty square miles of wide-open Aquitaine farmland.”

The order of battle

Narrator — “Deploying to the field are:

The 1st Brigade of Foot, under Bg. General @etirflita [Sir Marius Thibodeaux];

The Royal Foot Guards, under Marquis-Colonel @Curtis Sinnoch, with Lt. Col. @Jules Lavelle commanding the 1st Battalion;

Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons, under Lt. Col. @sirsfurther [Robert d’Engaux], with Cap. @DancingHorse [Primo Tremblay] leading F Troop;

Pvt. @CitrusNotes [Jerome Greybeck] serving in the Gascon Regiment’s E Company;

and Pvt. @WillWorker [Sébastien Lefebvre] attached to a very confused Frontier Regiment.”

The NPC commanders, in the Narrator’s own words:

  • Col. Melvin Duret, Frontier Regiment — “a nobleman of slightly too-certain breeding. (MA 2)”
  • Maj. Servais Poussin, Gascon Regiment 3rd Battalion — “stolid and unpretentious commander of the Gascon Regiment’s 3rd Battalion, leads his men with grim determination.”

The player characters in the line of battle:

Character Rank / Post Station
Sir Marius Thibodeaux Brigadier General, 1st Brigade of Foot commanding the Royal Marines and Picardy Musketeers
Curtis Sinnoch Marquis-Colonel, Royal Foot Guards commanding the regiment
Jules Lavelle Lt. Colonel, Royal Foot Guards commanding the 1st Battalion
Robert d’Engaux Lt. Colonel, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons commanding the regiment
Primo Tremblay Captain, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons leading F Troop
Jerome Greybeck Private, the Gascon Regiment E Company
Sébastien Lefebvre Private attached to the Frontier Regiment

Primo’s captaincy was two weeks old. He had purchased it in the off-season on the strength of borrowed glory — Robert d’Engaux, “in the blush of his amour,” had “started to speak fondly of the 69th and give Primo credit for a battle he never fought in.” As the player put it:

Primo Tremblay — “Primo was certainly not about to disabuse him of that notion. He shined his shoes, straightened his cap, decorated it with a lucky feather from Armadine’s cock, the Right Reverend Raphael Legoinegasque, and marched to the regimental house to apply and purchase a captaincy while the wind blew in his favor.”

The record

Narrator — “As the ranking officer in the field, @etirflita will detail the plan of deployment and roll.”

Sir Marius Thibodeaux — “The Royal Marines will be aiding in the siege effort to prevent the rejoining of the rebels and their leaders while the Picardy Musketeers will be systematically hunting the rebels in the nearby farmland.”

And, to the gentlemen back in Paris, from the streets that same hour:

Sir Marius Thibodeaux — “We shall be back in time to Christmas in Paris. I plan to toast to France with you fine chaps next month!”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux’s command roll: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “The 1st Brigade immediately makes contact with multiple enemy regiments. The Picardy Musketeers engage in pitched battle, and even manage to draw attention from the Royal Marines, who succeed in withdrawing to assist the siege. The Marines arrive just in time to rebuff a desperate sally by the defenders, successfully capturing George Villiers, the Duke of Buckingham himself, in the midst of an escape attempt.”

Narrator — “The result is 1 - Enemy crushed.”

Three French flags went up in the reactions. Then the Narrator turned to the Gascons, and the campaign found its name:

Narrator — “Maj. Servais Poussin, stolid and unpretentious commander of the Gascon Regiment’s 3rd Battalion, leads his men with grim determination. Spotting a line of enemy forces across a field of asparagus, he orders a charge.”

🎲 Maj. Poussin’s command roll: 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The enemy line is revealed to be a line of tactically-placed scarecrows. The 3rd Battalion opts to vent their frustrations on them anyway, screaming and bayoneting the silent guardians. They little notice the approach of a Huguenot force, who open fire from a mere 30 yards away, tearing huge holes in the line and sending the battalion scattering. The result is 6 - Own crushed.”

The Dragoons’ colonel saw the massacre from a distance and turned his regiment:

Robert d’Engaux — “Robert spots Poussin being ambushed and decides to intervene. His dragoons had been behind the 3rd battalion on their way to assist the siege, but Robert decides to take the opportunity to charge into the enemy while they are distracted.”

Meanwhile the Frontier Regiment got its orders:

Narrator — “Col. Melvin Duret orders the Frontier Regiment into an encircling maneuver. Encircling what exactly, he is not sure.”

🎲 Col. Duret’s command roll: 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The Frontier Regiment spreads out in a horseshoe shape throughout the farmland, thinly enough that they fail to encounter more than a few similarly disorganized enemy units. The result is 4 - Inconclusive.”

Robert’s rescue charge came to the roll:

🎲 Lt. Col. d’Engaux’s command roll: d6 — 3

Narrator — “By the time the Dragoons reach the 3rd Battalion, it is much too late. Scarecrows and soldiers are scattered like chaff, rather inappropriately for an asparagus field. The remaining Huguenots disperse, but the Dragoons are demoralized by their failure to save the footmen. The result is 4 - Inconclusive.”

The Royal Foot Guards took the field last, their Marquis-Colonel having drawn his own conclusions from the day’s botany:

Curtis Sinnoch — “After hearing of the dangers of Asparagus (And silently commenting that he would still prefer the stink of a Parisian back alley in Springtime then the odor of the English), Curtis deploys his men along a forest ridge following a well-traveled route, hoping to catch a caravan of Huguenots as they re-position themselves”

🎲 Marquis-Colonel Sinnoch’s command roll: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The Royal Foot Guards, disciplined as ever, maintain their positions and pick off the enemy at a distance wherever he positions himself. Charge after charge of Protestant valor is met and repulsed by the implacable force of Marquis-Colonel Sinnoch and his regiment. The result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

And the 1st Battalion under Jules Lavelle — whose choice of metaphor, given what had just happened to Private Lefebvre (see below), was in exquisitely poor taste:

Jules Lavelle — “This, truly, is no more difficult than a grouse hunt at my family’s estate in Brittany. I can simply lean back and let my skillful soldiers do what they do best.”

🎲 Lt. Col. Lavelle’s command roll: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “True to form, Jules continues the fine example set by his commander. The result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

The reckoning

Jerome Greybeck — survivor of the scarecrow charge

As Sebastien smugly observes the fallen bird, another of its kind pops its head out of the underbrush, softly cooing the last sound he will ever hear. His final thought, before being torn to shreds, is ‘petite futée…’”

The reactions tell the story: three doves, three crying-laughing faces, two stars, two screams, and a plank. A tombstone went up in the cemetery within the half hour. Sébastien’s player spent the rest of the resolution haunting the survivors’ rolls as a ghost — see below.

Primo Tremblay — the poltroon in the scarecrow

The verdict was pronounced from the GM’s own table account:

The close of the campaign

Narrator — “The battle is a great success, forcing the final field elements of the English-supported Huguenots back into the city. La Rochelle remains under siege, but with Cardinal Richelieu directing the construction of great siege engines, a firm naval blockade, and plans for further fortifications already in motion, the city’s fall is only a matter of time.”

The ledger of Fall 1627. The Duke of Buckingham — George Villiers himself — a prisoner of the Royal Marines. Sir Marius Thibodeaux: mentioned in despatches (+5 SP for 3 months, +1 thereafter), a 400-crown ducal ring off Buckingham’s finger, and a promotion with nowhere to go. Curtis Sinnoch: mentioned, and 800 crowns of caravan goods. Jules Lavelle: mentioned, 700 crowns, and a barony. Jerome Greybeck: promoted Subaltern out of the wreck of the scarecrow charge. Primo Tremblay: alive, disgraced, at −4 SP a month. The Gascon 3rd Battalion: crushed among the asparagus. Dead: Pvt. Sébastien Lefebvre, of grouse; Lt. Col. Robert d’Engaux, of principle.

The replacements were rolling in the chargen channel before the day was out. Robert’s player introduced two arrivals:

sirsfurther — “Two Fresh arrivals in Paris: one a wealthy heiress from Normandy, Marie d’Rouen (SL:15)” — “The other, the bastard spawn of the Earl of Ambroise and a washerwoman, Louis Laveuse”

And the next day, in the streets en passant, Sébastien’s player unveiled his own successor at the late Hercule Bonaparte’s manor — one Jean Shawinigan, a stranded time traveler making the best of the seventeenth century:

Jean Shawinigan — “So instead Jean Shawinigan will arrive in Paris and do the things young rich men do. Calculations are that the damage to the timeline will be minimal. But maybe I should damage the timeline? Tough choice: Life stuck as a wealthy gentleman in the 1600s or stuck in a temporal prison in the 2800s?”

Table talk

❦

Winter 1627–28 — The Baronets’ Raid on the King

December 1627 – February 1628 · resolved at the table 4–5 November 2021 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: the Norman bocage, on the road to Paris

The situation

With La Rochelle sealed under siege after the autumn’s fighting, the army marched home — and straight into an invasion aimed at the person of the King himself. On the night of 4 November the Narrator posted “Processing the campaign tonight,” and just after midnight opened the winter books:

Narrator — “The Winter Campaign”

Narrator — “With the Huguenots contained in La Rochelle, the bulk of the army is pulled back to Paris to defend the capital. This is fortuitous, as even with the English navy held off at sea, and Buckingham in chains, a new English threat has presented itself.

A swarm of new baronets, created by Charles V to bolster the officer corps of the military, have taken it upon themselves to seize King Louis himself. Landing in Normandy, the new lords and their household forces have made quick work of the meager northern coastal defenses and set inland. Thankfully, they lack common leadership, and have split up across the bocage to pillage and raid on their way to the capital.”

Narrator — “With the Cardinal overseeing the siege at La Rochelle, the King himself has condescended to order the army to defend his royal person at all costs. This will be a Field Operation.”

The order of battle

Narrator — “Forces Deploying:

-Bg. Gen. @etirflita in command of the 1st Brigade of Foot -Marquis Col. @Curtis Sinnoch in command of the Royal Foot Guards, including Lt. Col. @Jules Lavelle -Maj. @ArcticFox in command of the Crown Prince Cuirassiers 3rd Squadron -Cap. @DancingHorse leading the Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons C Troop -Sub. @CitrusNotes with the Frontier Regiment -@sirsfurther, without rank, who has been temporarily assigned the rank of Private in the Frontier Regiment”

The NPC commanders, in the Narrator’s own words:

  • Col. Gabriel Kaplan, Frontier Regiment — “once an able commander, now a blithering fool after taking a musket ball to the cranium.”
  • Col. Bertrand Beauvais, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons — who “recognizes the proper use of cavalry in a wide-ranging battle like this.”

The player characters in the line of battle:

Character Rank / Post Station
Marius Thibodeaux Brigadier General 1st Brigade of Foot (Picardy Musketeers, Royal Marines)
Curtis Sinnoch Marquis, Colonel Royal Foot Guards
Jules Lavelle Baron, Lieutenant Colonel 1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards
Jean Renault Major Crown Prince Cuirassiers 3rd Squadron, attached to the Frontier Regiment
Primo Tremblay Captain C Troop, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons
Jerome Greybeck Subaltern Frontier Regiment
Pierre d’Amboise Private (temporary) Frontier Regiment

Jerome Greybeck had volunteered himself for the season — and only worked out at the table what that meant:

CitrusNotes — “I am listed as with the frontier regiment, but I was able to pay my debts with the money Jean left with me” — “wait, no, I can’t volunteer the regiment, so if I volunteered (and I did) I’m going to Frontier” — “I get it”

Jean Renault, whose Cuirassiers had not deployed as a regiment, found his squadron folded under Col. Kaplan’s command — a question he raised with some foreboding:

Jean Renault — “Out of curiosity, why am I under the comand of the Frontier Regiment Commander as a separate divison?” — “Or was he that bad that he screwed it up for everyone?”

Narrator — “Technically squadrons/battalions can’t act independently, but need to be part of a regimental command structure” — “your regiment didn’t deploy. your squadron did, so you get to keep your command and the benefits of your regimental membership, but you still have to serve under the frontier regiment”

The record

Narrator — “As the senior officer on the field, it falls to @etirflita [Bg. Gen. Marius Thibodeaux] to provide the overall plan of battle.”

Narrator — “The enemy is loosely arranged in pockets scattered over the countryside. No frontage presents itself; the tall hedges separating one field from the next guarantee isolated fighting. The rising smoke and screaming peasants make it a trivial matter to find the enemy.”

Narrator — “@ArcticFox, @Curtis Sinnoch are invited to give their orders and roll as well.”

Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons — Col. Bertrand Beauvais

Narrator — “Col. Bertrand Beauvais of Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons recognizes the proper use of cavalry in a wide-ranging battle like this, and assigns his forces on a series of small skirmish and containment missions, refusing to directly engage the enemy.”

🎲 Col. Beauvais’s command roll: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “The plan works perfectly, and the enemy, once routed, finds they have nowhere to go. The result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

The Frontier Regiment — Col. Gabriel Kaplan

Narrator — “Col. Gabriel Kaplan rides up and down the line, gesticulating wildly in the direction of some hedges. Whatever he means to say is lost in a cascade of spittle that soaks his uniform. He attempts to make up for a lack of articulation with pure fighting spirit.”

🎲 Col. Kaplan’s command roll: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “He fails. He raises his sword, giving the order for the Frontier Regiment to charge the hedgerow. Of course, all his wild shouts have given plenty of warning to the English forces, who open fire from behind cover.”

Narrator — “The result is 5 - Own driven from field.”

Crown Prince Cuirassiers 3rd Squadron — Maj. Jean Renault

Jean Renault — “@Narrator My troupe and I will use cavalry skirmish tactics, hit and run attacks”

Narrator — “@ArcticFox go ahead and roll. Due to Col. Kaplan’s incompetence, you will receive a -1 to your MA for this calculation.”

🎲 Maj. Renault’s command roll: 1d6 — 2 (at −1 MA)

Narrator — “Jean’s attempt to skirmish with an enemy that refuses to enter the open field goes poorly. His riders are picked apart as they wheel back and forth in front of their hidden foes. The result is 6 - Own crushed.”

1st Brigade of Foot — Bg. Gen. Marius Thibodeaux

Marius Thibodeaux — “Let’s hunt some baronets! These traitors would do anything for power, so we shall show them the might of France’s armies! We will split up and engage the enemy in smaller forces to ensure none of these treacherous villains flee the field.”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux’s command roll: 1d6 — 3

Narrator — “@etirflita’s 1st Brigade scatters to meet the enemy on equal terms. The Picardy Musketeers make great headway, flushing out the enemy wherever he is found. The Royal Marines, unfortunately, flounder so far from shore, unable to operate independently in the thick underbrush. The result is 3 - Inconclusive.”

The Royal Foot Guards — Marquis Col. Curtis Sinnoch

Curtis Sinnoch — “Men! Our Duty here is to protect King Louis himself! We have a holy Duty and the glorious privilege of securing the very heart of France - We take the fields one by one, and clear the filthy English from our lands! Expect and give no quarter - to arms!”

🎲 Col. Sinnoch’s command roll: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “As usual, the Royal Foot Guards execute a series of textbook maneuvers, flushing out the enemies with feints, drawing them into battle with the anvil, and then bringing the other half of the regiment down as the hammer. The enemy are annihilated by those whose sworn mission is the King’s own safety. The result is 1 - Enemy crushed.”

1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards — Lt. Col. Jules Lavelle

Narrator — “@Jules Lavelle, give your orders and roll - you get a +1 to MA for your commander’s stellar leadership.”

Jules Lavelle — “There is little to do but submit to the genius of Col. Sinnoch. 1st Battalion, set them up and knock them down!”

🎲 Lt. Col. Lavelle’s command roll: 1d6 — 6 (at +1 MA)

Narrator — “Whatever forces remain intact after the bulk of the regiment makes contact, the 1st Battalion soon scatters. The result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

The reckoning

Jerome Greybeck — Subaltern, Frontier Regiment

Narrator — “@CitrusNotes, your rolls are Death 7, Mention 10, Promotion 7, Crowns 10.”

🎲 Jerome Greybeck rolls 1d6 Death — 2

Narrator — “Jerome Greybeck, eager to prove himself, leads the charge. His big moment has finally arrived. The biggest gamble of his life is about to pay off.”

Narrator — “With his comrades dropping like flies around him, he manages to enter the hedgerow itself, only to be tangled in its vines. The treacherous Norman countryside has him in its grips. He makes easy pickings for the English bayonets.”

The player gave his man one last flourish:

CitrusNotes — “Bleeding profusely and losing all sense, Jerome sees a small vine of ripe berries growing at his feet. One last meal, he thinks as he weakly scoops a few into his mouth. A final throw off the dice. They were, of course, poisonous, but you must have blood in your body for that to matter…”

Primo Tremblay — Captain, C Troop, Princess Louisa’s Light Dragoons

Narrator — “@DancingHorse, your rolls are Death 11, Mention 10, Promotion 9, Crowns 8.”

Primo had spent the autumn hiding in a scarecrow and the months since under a −4 SP cloud of disgrace. This winter he chose redemption:

Primo Tremblay — “Primo, has taken to hard living for the past few months and practiced an ascetic regime after experiencing the loss of his lady love as well as his social standing due to his cowardice. Cowardice, which, he reasoned, came from soft city living. Now, hardened, and with his soul right with God, he takes Reckless Bravery for 2. He leads the Dragoons on, and is the first to dismount and engage an isolated pocket of enemies.”

🎲 Primo Tremblay rolls 2d6 death — 9 (Death target 11, less 2 for Reckless Bravery: he dies)

Narrator — “Primo feels confident under such a seasoned commander. He holds his place aside his horse without the fear that has haunted him before. He is where he is meant to be. Even as the sharp crack of musket fire heralds the approaching enemy, he steadies his pistol and readies for the fight.”

Narrator — “Primo’s horse is less ready. As the volley of incoming fire spatters harmlessly into the ground before the waiting dragoons, his horse spooks, and turns to run. In the process, it catches a stirrup against Primo’s sword, pulling him to the ground and dragging him behind into a field of waiting thistles.”

Narrator — “What returns to camp, still dragged behind the horse, is not recognizable as a soldier or a man, but the grooms know the horse well.”

Two fresh tombstones went up in the cemetery channel within the hour — Greybeck’s at 1:00 AM, Tremblay’s at 1:08. By 1:16 AM CitrusNotes was already rolling 3d6 for his next man, the dice bot still answering under the dead man’s name: “Jerome Greybeck: An error was thrown when evaluating your expression…”

Jean Renault — Major, Crown Prince Cuirassiers 3rd Squadron

The close of the campaign

Narrator — “The campaign has concluded. Some have fallen, but more have found great glory, and the King’s person is assuredly safe once more. The upstart English nobles slip back to the coast, having learned their lesson about French force-of-arms.”

The butcher’s bill: two player characters dead — Subaltern Jerome Greybeck, bayoneted in a hedgerow with poisonous berries on his lips, and Captain Primo Tremblay, dragged to pieces behind his own horse, his disgrace never redeemed. The honors: a mention and a full lieutenant-colonelcy for Jean Renault; a mention and 200 crowns for Marquis Col. Sinnoch; converted-promotion mentions for Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux (+150 crowns) and Lt. Col. Lavelle (+800 crowns); 100 crowns for Private d’Amboise. Col. Kaplan, spittle-soaked author of the Frontier Regiment’s rout, survived to command again.

Spring 1628 — The Slaughter on the Beaches

March – May 1628 · resolved at the table 13–14 December 2021 · Mission: Field Operations · Theater: the landing beaches, dunes, and coastal forests near La Rochelle

The situation

The bloodiest single afternoon of the whole war — four player characters dead in one resolution, two of them by the same cannonball. On 13 December the Narrator opened the spring books:

Narrator — “The Spring Campaign”

Narrator — “As the siege of La Rochelle proceeds, the English show no signs of abandoning their attempts to relieve the city. Week after week, ships of all sizes attempt to slip through the blockade, and enough succeed that the city continues to maintain its resistance, despite all odds.”

Narrator — “Spies in Cornwall have reported an unusual gathering of fishing boats backed up by the English Admiralty. The newly created office of the First Lord of the Admiralty, held by the newly-elevated Baron of Nayland, has put together plans for a multi-prong distraction and resupply mission. While the French military would normally be hard-pressed to resist such a concentration, even without English army support, the volunteers out of Paris have been unusually enthusiastic this season, and the Cardinal orders the hodgepodge of regiments deployed in force.”

“Unusually enthusiastic” was no exaggeration. In the days before the resolution the volunteers had lined up cheerfully:

WillWorker — “Ding to SL7 and Volunteer.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Volunteer for war!”

Jean Renault — “VOLUNTEER” — “TO WAR WE GO”

The enemy, in the Narrator’s own words:

Narrator — “The enemy forces are primarily English sea dogs, hearty men who’ve cut their teeth battling pirates in the Caribbean, many of whom saw their first service under Sir Francis Drake himself.”

The order of battle

Narrator — “The French forces include:

Bg. General @etirflita commanding the Royal Northern Highland Border regiment.

Marquis Col. @Curtis Sinnoch, commanding the Royal Foot Guards, with Baron Lt. Col. @Jules Lavelle commanding the 1st Battalion, and Pvt. @sirsfurther serving in C Company.

Pvt. @Slade Imbee, serving in the King’s Musketeers F Company under Lt. Col. Adhemar Lucy (MA 5).

Col. Raoul LaRue (MA 2), commanding the Frontier Regiment, with Lt. Col. @ArcticFox and his Crown Prince Cuirassiers 1st Squadron, and Cpts. @WillWorker and @DancingHorse leading companies.”

Narrator — “Correction: @etirflita commands a battalion under Col. Matthieu Barrault (MA 4).”

Narrator — “Col. LaRue agrees to follow Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux’s advice in all matters.”

The player characters in the line of battle:

Character Rank / Post Station
Marius Thibodeaux Brigadier General battalion of the Royal Northern Highland Border regiment, under Col. Matthieu Barrault (MA 4)
Curtis Sinnoch Marquis, Colonel Royal Foot Guards — senior officer, author of the deployment plan
Jules Lavelle Baron, Lieutenant Colonel 1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards
Pierre d’Amboise Private C Company, Royal Foot Guards
Slade Imbee Private F Company, King’s Musketeers, under Lt. Col. Adhemar Lucy (MA 5)
Jean Renault Lieutenant Colonel Crown Prince Cuirassiers 1st Squadron, attached to Col. Raoul LaRue’s Frontier Regiment (MA 2)
Jean Shawinigan Captain (E Troop, Grand Duke Max’s Dragoons) leading a company under Col. LaRue
Anton Dechets Captain (Gascon Regiment) leading a company under Col. LaRue

Two of the men marching to the coast were new recruits of the winter, and their enlistments read like prophecy. Pierre d’Amboise had applied to the Royal Foot Guards in November, and Col. Sinnoch himself conducted the interview:

Pierre d’Amboise — “Monsieur, for many years I have devoted myself to the study of books and laws. I have left the fighting of wars to others. But now I have seen the field of battle, and though it is a terrible thing, I cannot take my mind away from the glory to be found there in the service of France. I understand at last why soldiers boast of their deeds on the field and I wish to share in their boasts!”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Ah man of letters, are you? Books and laws are all fine, but understanding military history - such is the true value of the written word! To read orders and missives, to understand what is happening both at home and abroad, is a mark of true command! @sirsfurther - we will gladly take you and your letters. Be prepared - any cowardly behavior will be dealt with extreme prejudice - we shall not sully the honor of France!”

Narrator — “@sirsfurther, purchasing a commission?”

Pierre d’Amboise — “Most certainly, I shall purchase it with the blood of the King’s enemies on the field of battle!”

Jean Renault — “Or die trying!”

Anton Dechets had had a rougher road to the colors — his first application drew the Narrator’s verdict “Rejected even by the Gascon Regiment. There is nowhere lower to apply.” — before a December re-roll got him in and he bought a captaincy:

Anton Dechets — “Anton considers using water and soap to clean up before applying, but decides against it, simply brushing himself off and finding a feather to put in his hat.”

The record

🎲 The Narrator’s situation roll: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “By the time the French forces have made the march from Paris, the English have already made their landing. They’ve landed on beaches up and down the coast, and are transporting convoys of goods overland to reach the city, the sea routes being too heavily defended. It falls to the regimental commanders to find a plan to clear the beaches, brush, and coastal forests of the enemy.”

Narrator — “As the ranking officer, @etirflita should lay out the plan of deployment with @Curtis Sinnoch advising.”

Then, with the correction that Thibodeaux commanded only a battalion, the plan passed to the Marquis:

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch, the deployment plan is up to you.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Overall deployment of all forces, or just the RFG?”

Narrator — “Overall – the other colonels are not bound by it, but will respect your seniority.”

The deployment plan — Marquis Col. Curtis Sinnoch

Curtis Sinnoch — “My glorious compatriots and men-at-arms! Once again, the villainous English are attempting to subvert France herself and corrupt our people! We will show them no quarter, nor should we expect any! The Royal Foot Guard will set up in the forests along with the King’s Musketeers - we will ambush and pick off any unexpecting forces. Baron Lt. Col, @Jules Lavelle, be on eye in particular for any caravans transporting weapons and ammunition - you have full permission to engage with extreme force as you see fit.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Col. LaRue - if your men can harass the English as they unload on the beaches - set upon them before they can defend themselves, and then withdraw to the dunes and forests when they attempt to counter, we will have Lt. Col. @ArcticFox’s Calvary ready to tear them apart with a counter charge.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Col. Matthieu Barrault, your fine soldiers are some of the best France has to offer. With the might and brains of @etirflita, I believe you have the ability to break any force the Sea Dogs launch at us. Whereas the Frontier Regiment will operate as a hammer throwing their ranks into discord, you shall operate as a scalpel, sabotaging landing spots, eliminating officers, and engaging in any targets of opportunity you may find - my only request is to arrange with captains @WillWorker and @DancingHorse any beach operations you perform to make sure we have an adequate rearguard in the event they attempt any trickery of their own. TO THE GLORY OF FRANCE!”

The Royal Foot Guards — Marquis Col. Curtis Sinnoch

🎲 Col. Sinnoch’s command roll: 1d6 — 4

Narrator — “The French forces follow their orders nearly to the letter, repulsing the enemy wherever they encounter him. There are losses, yes, but these are the glorious deaths of battle, not the shameful ones of cowardice. Curtis sits atop his horse, repressing a smirk, as every successive explosion and cheer heralds French triumph, and the English dogs returning to the sea that whelped them. Your result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

1st Battalion, Royal Foot Guards — Baron Lt. Col. Jules Lavelle

Jules Lavelle — “Far be it from me to let my commander down. Men, see to it not a single ounce of goods travels past us without keen inspection and attack if necessary! I don’t care if it’s some maman’s laundry, it will be bayonetted and burned for the glory of France!”

🎲 Lt. Col. Lavelle’s command roll: 1d6 — 6

Narrator — “Rather than some maman’s laundry, every inspection yields a cadre of slovenly, desperately attempting to sneak through the lines! Their subterfuge unveiled, they are cut apart with alacrity but the perennially well-drilled RFG. Your result is 2 - Enemy driven from field.”

The Royal Northern Highland Border regiment — Col. Matthieu Barrault

Narrator — “Col. Matthieu Barrault leads his forces on a frantic charge directly across the enemy lines, wherever they are found.”

🎲 Col. Barrault’s command roll: 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “The attempt is disastrous. Without intelligence from scouts, across strange terrain, the RNHB encounters traps, ambush, and an unfathomable amount of mud. The result is 5 - Own driven from field.”

Thibodeaux’s battalion, RNHB — Bg. Gen. Marius Thibodeaux

Narrator — “@etirflita, give your orders and roll. You will receive a -1 to MA for your commander’s poor result.”

Marius Thibodeaux — “We will be hunting officers, but let us make some sport of it! Each rank is assigned points, whoever has the highest tally at the end of the onslaught will be toasted at the Blue Gables next month!”

🎲 Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux’s command roll: 1d6 — 2 (at −1 MA)

Narrator — “The Sea Dogs meet you en masse, as if they were expecting just such a foolhardy assault. Or perhaps this is simply their only approach to battle. At any rate, for every one of theirs, one or more of your forces fall. You battle each other to an uneasy standstill, and only the successes elsewhere on the field stave off complete disaster. Your result is 4 - Inconclusive.”

The King’s Musketeers — Lt. Col. Adhemar Lucy

Narrator — “Lt. Col Adhemar Lucy, following the exemplary conduct of the RFGs, watches Sinnoch’s signals and gives the exact same orders to his own men.”

🎲 Lt. Col. Lucy’s command roll: 1d6 — 5

Narrator — “It leads to the exact same result, stirring victory (BR 2).”

The Frontier Regiment — Col. Raoul LaRue

Narrator — “Col. Raoul LaRue attempts to support the RNHB from behind, spreading out his forces to seize the enemy boats and deny them transport back across the sea, while providing a fallback position in case the more surgical strikes go wrong.”

🎲 Col. LaRue’s command roll: 1d6 — 1

Narrator — “After noting the scattered boats on the shore, the Frontier Regiment keeps their eyes firmly fixed inland. They don’t notice the special unit of Reavers, echoing the Northmen of old, rising out of the surf. The Reavers slit the throats of the entire rear ranks before an alarm can even be raised. The fighting is fierce, horrific, and brief. The Frontier Regiment is shattered. The result is 6 - Own crushed.”

Crown Prince Cuirassiers 1st Squadron — Lt. Col. Jean Renault

Narrator — “@ArcticFox, you will receive a -1 to MA for determining your battle result due to your commander’s awful showing.”

Jean Renault — “Oof”

Jean Renault — “As calvary, we are not prepared to fight on the beaches, we will conduct skirmish hit and runs, harassing the enemy and attempting to allow our countrymen to continue the seige!”

🎲 Lt. Col. Renault’s command roll: 1d6 — 6 (at −1 MA)

Narrator — “Away from the beaches, your squadron handles itself somewhat better, though it struggles to corner the enemy, who keep retreating into the woods when you approach. Your men keep trying to find a siege to break, but there’s just woods and field here, and they whirl around in confusion, occasionally firing or being fired upon, without much result. 3 - Inconclusive.”

The two captains under LaRue had no command rolls of their own to make:

WillWorker — “@Narrator No battle roll for me?”

Narrator — “Captains don’t have a command” — “You just inherit a result from your colonel”

The reckoning

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch, your rolls are Death 15, Mention 10, Promotion 10, Crowns 5”

Jean Renault — “What are my rolls?”

Narrator — “wait your turn”

Jean Renault — “Just making sure”

Curtis Sinnoch — “We men of France lead by example - Reckless Bravery 2!” — “For Life!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 7 (Death target 15, less 2 for Reckless Bravery: survives)

Curtis Sinnoch — “For Mention!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 6 (no mention)

Curtis Sinnoch — “For Promotion!”

🎲 Curtis Sinnoch rolls 2d6 — 8 (Promotion target 10, less 2 for Reckless Bravery: promoted)

Narrator — “The boats are found to be brimming with pirate booty. 200 crowns’ worth of Spanish doubloons are delivered to you.”

“As there are no open positions” — those words would not stay true for another quarter of an hour.

Narrator — “@Jules Lavelle, your rolls are Death 15, Mention 10, Promotion 10, Crowns 5”

Jules Lavelle — “I shall show my commander the meaning of courage. Reckless Bravery 3!”

Narrator — “700 crowns are yours for the taking, as you skim the cream off the top before handing it up the chain.”

Marius Thibodeaux — Brigadier General, RNHB — the galleon

Narrator — “@etirflita, your rolls are Death 10, Mention 10, Promotion 5, Crowns 5”

Marius Thibodeaux — “Reckless Bravery 2!”

🎲 Marius Thibodeaux rolls 2d6 death — 9 (Death target 10, less 2 for Reckless Bravery: he dies)

Five grim-face reactions and three planks piled onto the roll. Five minutes of silence, then a single word from the player:

etirflita — “Angélique.....”

Narrator — “The English lack any of the polish or refinement of the French, battling like madmen, stinking of rum and their own excrement as they come out of the woods in endless lines, gap-toothed grins and wide eyes the last sight many of the gallant French soldiers see. Not Bg. Gen. Thibodeaux, though, who leads a fighting retreat back to the beach, shooting and stabbing his way from horseback, carving a bloody path even through the line of Reavers who stand basking in their slaughter of the Frontier Regiment.

Finally given room to breathe, Thibodeaux sets his back to the sea. His horse covered in lather, he sees the hordes of hideous English preparing to deliver him to his maker. The Brigadier-General and his companions realize this is their moment, and set to charge. To their shock, as one, the reavers turn and flee inland. Thibodeaux and the other survivors realize that French valor has cowed their feral foe once again.

They never see the galleon rise up out of the surf behind them, carried high on a freak wave, slamming down into the gathered Frenchmen below. Onlookers report a loud slapping sound of wood on sand, and then eerie silence.”

Marius Thibodeaux had been at the table for over a year — his player’s fifth and longest-lived character, risen all the way to Brigadier General, crushed flat under a ship. And his star had no sooner set than the vacancy opened:

Narrator — “@Curtis Sinnoch, you may take your promotion instead of the mention now, if you wish.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Curtis, watching through a spyglass several kilometers away, watches as the galleon smash into the sand and erupt into the base planks and boards that formerly composed a ship. He sighs, shakes his head ruefully, then straightens”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Send a message to his Grace the Cardinal. While we have won this day, there has been… a vacancy that needs to be addressed. We need to ensure it goes to a man of proper upbringing and valor to honor the memory of good Thibodeaux”

Narrator — “The Cardinal concludes that a Marquis outranks a Knight, and passes his recommendation to the Minister of War, who orders the promotion to Brevet Brigadier-General for Marquis Sinnoch.”

Jean Renault — Lieutenant Colonel, Crown Prince Cuirassiers 1st Squadron

Narrator — “@ArcticFox, your rolls are Death 10, Mention 12, Promotion 9, Crowns 7”

etirflita — “What’s the worst that could happen?”

Renault’s first roll went out on the wrong dice — an error nobody caught until after the loot was counted.

Jean Renault — “For mention!”

🎲 Jean Renault rolls 2d6 — 10 (Mention target 12: no mention)

Jean Renault — “I hear there are promotions open!”

🎲 Jean Renault rolls 2d6 — 5 (Promotion target 9: no promotion)

Jean Renault — “But not for me this day!” — “But maybe there’s loot?”

Narrator — “While taking a break from riding in circles, your men notice a sparkling ring on the side of the road, and hand it to you. It’s worth 100 crowns.”

Narrator — “wait, @ArcticFox, did you properly roll 2d6 for death?”

Jean Renault — “I thought I did” — “Want I should re-roll it?” — “I dont see the addition symbols”

Narrator — “it looks like you didn’t roll at all”

🎲 Jean Renault rolls 2d6 — 4 (Death target 10: survives)

Narrator — “yeah you typed it wrong” — “ok you’re alive”

Jean Renault — “haha” — “Phew”

Jean Shawinigan — Captain, under Col. LaRue — the temporal paradox

Narrator — “While we wait, @WillWorker, your rolls are Death 6, Mention 7, Promotion 6. No chance for loot.”

The player answered the ugly numbers with the fullest confession his time-traveling character ever made:

Jean Shawinigan — “Jean knew going to war was a risk, but this wasn’t the first conflict he’d been tasked with observing. And the potential benefit to securing a better and more comfortable existence in this barbaric hellhole he was stranded in seemed worth it.

However, as Jean found out, it is one thing to be a temporal observer of a war and another thing entirely to be a participant. As soon as he stepped onto the field of battle, the temporal database went berserk. With the butterfly effect wreaking its havoc and Jean witnessing Frenchman everywhere laying down their lives for a hard won fight, the probability map for the outcome as a costless victory for the French dropped from Reasonable Certainty to Flux.

All bets were now off. Both for his survival and that of the version of the future he came from. Records from the 1600s were sparse for details as to how events actually unfolded. And it could merely be that the historical records were wrong. The timeline may be fine and the bloodless accounts from this era were simply the result of a bias by those who wrote the ending.

But with the battle engaged, there was no time to determine if and how his presence could alter things so significantly. Survive first. Fix later.

‘Alright men, follow my lead,’ Jean shouted, ‘Our charge will take us perilously close to the enemy before we dismount. In fact, I encourage you all to plow your horse into the enemy as you leap from the saddle.’

With that, Jean led the charge, hoping an overwhelming show of devastation would demoralize the enemy into leaving him be. As he closed in on the carnage, Jean used his enhanced strength and agility to leap from the saddle. While his horse trampled over two men, his landing coincided with his blade cutting down another Englishman who was too dumbstruck by the scene to react. The battle joined, all that was left was for the time traveler to hope fate would favor him.”

🎲 Jean Shawinigan rolls 2d6 Death — 9 (Death target 6: he dies)

Narrator — “The fighting is awful, far worse than any entertainment or historical reenactment could have prepared him for. Jean kills, and kills again, and soon his vision is nothing but metal and blood, and his mind focuses on nothing more than the endless, repetitive cycle of dodging, cutting, and trying to ignore the awful stench.

His awareness returns as the rank-and-file drop away and he finds himself squaring off with an enemy captain, a man of uncommon cleanliness compared to the rest of the ragged foe. He bears medals and other decorations on his sailing coat, and strikes with a finesse and lightness uncharacteristic of the savage cutlass blows of his fellows.

Jean and the Englishman strike back and forth at each other, making little progress, until Jean sees an opening. Allowing his opponent to overcommit, he steps on the man’s foot and shoves with his shoulder, stopping his opponent from catching himself and knocking him to the ground. He kicks the young captain’s sword loose, then raises his own blade for the coup de grace.

Reports from nearby soldiers are scattered and confused as to what came next. All agree, though, that there was a bright light, as if from nowhere, and strange words warning of ‘temporal paradox’. A moment later, Jean’s body seemed to vaporize, exploding into particles of quickly-dissipating light that swirled up into the sky, leaving nothing behind but an astonished English captain.”

Pierre d’Amboise — Private, C Company, Royal Foot Guards

Narrator — “@sirsfurther, your rolls are Death 13, Mention 10, Promotion 10, Crowns 7.”

Pierre d’Amboise — “As the Royal Foot Guards march towards the battle, Pierre feels that everything looks all too familliar. He ruminates on this strange feeling for a long time before it dawns on him that this was the exact situation which faced General Antonius during the Sicilian Revolt of 268. After a moment or two of contemplation, he recalls the various crucial mistakes which Antonius made on that occassion.

‘Oh dear, I must warn Curtis’ he mutters to himself.

After glancing one way and then the other to check that there aren’t any sergants nearby to catch him, he dashes off the road and into the bushes, in the vague direction which he imagined the Colonel to be.

Unfortunately for Pierre, he bumps directly into Sergant Fellipe.

‘Right sonny, trying to desert are we? I know just what to do with you.’

Just as Pierre begins the explain the similarity between the present situation and that of 268, and the consequent necessity of protecting the army’s left flank, the sergant wallops Pierre about the face and drags him to the front of the line.

‘Right sonny, now you can go where the real fighting is’”

sirsfurther — “Pierre, involuntarily, uses reckless bravery 3”

🎲 Pierre d’Amboise rolls 2d6 — 10 (Death target 13, less 3 for involuntary Reckless Bravery: he dies)

sirsfurther — “Oh well”

The table briefly tried to save him with arithmetic:

CitrusNotes — “Aren’t you still alive? You failed to succeed your death role” — “Your death is 13, you rolled 10, you’re good”

sirsfurther — “Reckless bravery 3”

ArcticFox — “He chose to be brave”

sirsfurther — “Can’t believe he didnt read my wall of text”

CitrusNotes — “Oooooo, I did read the flavor, I just neglected to do any math”

Pierre’s actual death waited on the Narrator’s pen — it arrived ninety minutes later, riding on someone else’s cannonball.

Anton Dechets — Captain, Gascon Regiment — the smuggler’s cannon

Narrator — “@DancingHorse, your rolls are Death 6, Mention 7, Promotion 6. No chance for loot.”

Anton Dechets — “Anton looks around. The battlefield is…an utter trash heap of misery and all the worst parts of humanity. It feels like he belongs, for some inexplicable reason. With a faint smile, his heart swells and he sidles into battle.”

Anton Dechets — “Reckless Beverly for 3!”

🎲 Anton Dechets rolls 2d6 death — 6 (Death target 6, less 3 for Reckless Bravery: he dies)

Narrator — “Anton somehow escapes the slaughter of his new allies in the penal battalion, fighting through to an opening in the lines of reavers, and from there making his way up the beach. Determined to find some other French unit to attach himself to, he is drawn to the sound of whispered French voices.

A thousand paces up the beach, he finds a small lagoon set back from a little shoal, almost totally hidden by tree cover. Only the sound of voices leads him to find the cove with its little skiff loaded with crates and barrels. The peasants working the docks seem shocked to see him.

Quickly examining the goods, Anton sees they’re a mix of English and French origin, all military materiél. ‘Looters…’ he utters, as the burly peasants serving as the muscle approach, wood cudgels in hand.

Anton falls back, bringing up his musket wildly, and gets off a shot, taking off the crown of the lead peasant. The rest scatter at the sound, leaving Anton to investigate the little smuggler’s dock on his own.

Creeping forward, he steps onto the little skiff, finding his sea legs as it rocks back and forth. One crate seems larger than the others, its corners marked with red paint. Curious, he grabs the edge, pulling softly.

There is a click and a hiss, and the box unfolds to reveal a smuggler’s cannon, rigged to go off and pointing straight at him. He tries to replace the cover, and there is a great explosion.

The cannonball travels through his chest, soaring out over the beach, streaked red and white with his blood and bones. Three hundred meters away, the Sergant shoves Pierre forward towards the front line. A random cannonball sails into the space just then occupied by his head, and removes it.

The battle continues much as it had before.”

One cannonball, two player characters. Anton Dechets and Pierre d’Amboise — the fourth and third deaths of the afternoon — share a single sentence of narration between them.

Slade Imbee — Private, F Company, King’s Musketeers

Narrator — “@Slade Imbee, your rolls are Death 12, Mention 9, Promotion 9, Crowns 8”

Slade rolled the next evening, the only man of the rank and file to walk off the beaches with a step up.

🎲 Slade Imbee rolls 2d6 — 8 (Death target 12: survives)

Slade Imbee — “Truly the world is a horrid place Slade thinks to himself while surveying the landscape. This carnage is nothing like the skirmishes in the frontier I yet I know I did not have the worst of it, @Curtis Sinnoch had us in the forests, a far better position than the beach, which I’ve heard is soaked red

He continues his careful trek through the woods, his fellows musketeers at his side, seeking to cut off straggling enemies from the larger forces. All are anxious not to draw attention… until they are ready.”

Narrator — “Survived! Roll for the rest”

🎲 Slade Imbee rolls 2d6 — 4 (Mention target 9: no mention) 🎲 Slade Imbee rolls 2d6 promotion — 12 (Promotion target 9: promoted) 🎲 Slade Imbee rolls 2d6 crowns — 9 (Crowns target 8: loot)

Narrator — “Promoted!” — “Congratulations to the newest subaltern in the King’s Musketeers.” — “Roll 2d6 for loot”

🎲 Slade Imbee rolls 2d6 loot — 2

Narrator — “200 crowns are delivered to you from the plunder!”

The butcher’s bill and the aftermath

Four player characters dead in a single resolution — the worst day of the entire war: Brigadier General Marius Thibodeaux, flattened under a galleon on a freak wave; Captain Jean Shawinigan, vaporized by his own future; Captain Anton Dechets, chest-shot by a booby-trapped smuggler’s cannon; Private Pierre d’Amboise, beheaded by the same ball three hundred meters on. The Frontier Regiment was shattered by the Reavers, its rear ranks with their throats slit in the surf. Against that: a Brevet Brigadier-Generalcy, a mention, and 200 crowns of Spanish doubloons for Marquis Sinnoch; 700 skimmed crowns for Lt. Col. Lavelle; a 100-crown roadside ring for Lt. Col. Renault; and a subaltern’s commission and 200 crowns for Slade Imbee. Four tombstones went up in the cemetery channel on the morning of 15 December, one after another between 12:35 and 12:39.

The survivors and successors took to the streets of Paris in the weeks that followed. Armand Dufour went looking for his one friend in the city:

Armand Dufour — “After spending yet more nights scouring his father’s letters for a lead in the mystery of his disappearance, Armand sets out on the town to connect again with the one solid contact he has managed to make in this manic place. Yet… no one seems to know his location, let alone even admit they ever even knew a Jean Shawinigan. But for a rambling vagrant on the street, who happily chattered on about the rise of ‘Great Jean the Timeless’ who bested forty men before ascending to a higher temporal plane, it was as if the man had never existed.”

Curtis Sinnoch — “Curtis stood at his mirror, admiring the new Bg. General insignia. It didn’t match his complexion the way the Royal Foot Guards did, but such is the price of duty… and promotion. He straightened his uniform again, and with a snap of his fingers summoned two servers ‘Send for my tailor. Now. It appears the continued rigors of battle have shrunk the sleeves of my tunic - we can’t have a general office looking unkempt, can we?’”

Pierre d’Amboise’s player marched his next man into Paris before the tombstones were even posted, ending the introduction with a question the beaches had already answered once:

sirsfurther — “At last, he has completed his journey. Will he find his mother on the streets of Paris? Will he discover the identity of his true father? Or will he be dashed to pieces in a foolish brawl or on the field of a foreign land?”

And Marius’s player walked a new pauper in off the carriage from the provinces:

etirflita — “Ah, Paris! The city in which I will die a pauper! My father runs a restaurant of which you may have heard, Le Maison de Gaufre? Well, it’s a franchise, he doesn’t own the whole chain, but he’s practically a manager! Anyway, he skimmed some of the nightly deposit to scrounge up enough carriage fare for me to come here and make it big!”

On 15 December the Minister of War rolled up the next season:

Narrator — “The Minister of War decides the composition of forces for the summer campaign” — “A single field army will be deployed, with every brigade except the Horse Guards and the 1st Brigade of Foot.” — “This will be a Siege campaign.”
